==PAGE:4==
ii
==PAGE:5==
Summary
The initial and the final setting of two different self-compacting concretes, called A35
and M30, which are typically used for vertical castings in Denmark, have been deter-
mined using different methods: The Vicat Needle Test, the Proctor Penetration Test,
the Knead Bag Method, and based on the compressive strength of cast cubes. Only
the Vicat and Proctor methods are applicable for determining initial setting. The mean
difference between the results using the two methods are 59 maturity minutes for the
A35 and 99 maturity minutes for the M30 where the Proctor method showed the latest
setting time. Regarding final setting the four different methods showed a varying result
and no tendency was to be seen between the methods.
An almost perfect correlation of R2=0.999 was found between a new method where final
setting is determined based on measurements of the self-compacting concrete‚Äôs heat de-
velopment and the final setting determined by the Proctor Penetration Test. This new
method enables an easier way to determine the setting time compared to the existing
commonly used methods.
The formwork pressure of a 6.0 meters tall wall, cast with the A35 self-compacting con-
crete from the top at a rate of 3.6 m/h, was measured. The experiment showed that
the formwork pressure was reduced to zero before any of the setting time methods had
determined initial or final setting. The maximum measured formwork pressure reached
65% of the hydrostatical pressure. Five different methods of calculating the maximum
formwork pressure were used: ACI 347R-04 and the method from [Anl√¶gsteknikforenin-
gen (2011)] both showed unfit for formwork pressure calculations when casting with
self-compacting concrete. The method from CIRIA report 108:1985 might be fit but
further studies are needed in order to prove this. The most commonly used method in
Denmark, the German standard DIN 18218, showed a result higher than hydrostatical
pressure, in which case the standard prescribes the use of hydrostatical pressure, and
the fifth method which were using hydrostatical pressure also showed a higher maximum
formwork pressure than measured. Based on the measurements of the formwork pressure
a formula was derived which can calculate the maximum formwork pressure based on
the parameters density of the concrete, rate of casting, and height of casting. Before it
can be concluded whether it can be generally adapted or not, further studies are needed.
In any case the present work shows there is found a potential for a more precise method
of calculating formwork pressure on walls when using self-compacting concrete.

Resum√©
Den begyndende og endelige afbindingstid for to forskellige selvkompakterende betoner,
kaldet A35 og M30, som er de mest anvendte i vertikale st√łbninger i Danmark, er
blevet bestemt med fire forskellige metoder: Vicat test, Proctor Penetrations test, Pose-
metoden samt baseret p√• trykstyrken af st√łbte kuber. Kun Vicat- og Proctormetoden
kan anvendes til at bestemme den begyndende afbindingstid. Proctormetoden resul-
terede i en gennemsnitlig senere bestemmelse af begyndende afbinding p√• 59 moden-
hedsminutter for A35 og 99 modenhedsminutter for M30. Hvad ang√•r endelige afbind-
ingstider bestemt ud fra de fire metoder, var der ingen tendenser mellem metoderne og
de meget varierende resultater.
En n√¶sten perfekt sammenh√¶ng lig med R2=0,999 blev fundet mellem en ny metode
hvor endelig afbinding bestemmes ud fra betonens varmeudvikling og endelig afbinding
fundet ved Proctormetoden. Denne nye metode g√łr bestemmelsen af sluttende afbinding
lettere end med de eksisterende metoder.
Formtrykket fra en 6,0 meter h√łj v√¶g, st√łbt ovenfra med den selvkompakterende be-
ton A35, med en st√łbehastighed p√• 3,6 m/t, blev m√•lt. Fors√łget viste, at formtrykket
var reduceret til nul, inden nogen af metoderne til bestemmelse af afbindingstider viste
begyndende eller endelig afbinding. Det maksimale formtryk blev m√•lt til 65% af det
hydrostatiske tryk. Fem forskellige metoder til bestemmelse af det maksimale formtryk
blev brugt: ACI 347R-04 og metoden fra [Anl√¶gsteknikforeningen (2011)] viste sig at
v√¶re ubrugelige til formtryksberegninger med selvkompakterende beton. Metoden fra
CIRIA rapport 108:1985 er muligvis retvisende, men yderligere studier er n√łdvendige
for at fastsl√• dette med sikkerhed. Den mest benyttede metode i Danmark, den tyske
DIN 18218, resulterede i et tryk der l√• over det hydrostatiske tryk, i hvilket tilf√¶lde
standarden foreskriver man da benytter det hydrostatiske tryk. Den femte metode er at
benytte hydrostatisk tryk, hvilket ogs√• l√• over det m√•lte.
Ud fra formtryksm√•lingerne blev en ny formel opstillet til bestemmelse af det maksimale
formtryk der tager h√łjde for b√•de den selvkompakterende betons densitet, st√łbehastighe-
den og st√łbeh√łjden. Der er dog brug for yderligere unders√łgelser af denne formel f√łr
den med sikkerhed kan konkluderes at v√¶re retvisende. Uanset hvad viser dette speciale
et potentiale til en nyt√¶nkning af formtryksberegningerne n√•r der bruges selvkompak-
terende beton.

Acknowledgements
Working with this Master‚Äôs thesis we had the privilege of being able to include some of
the most influential companies and research institutions in the Dansih civil engineering
field of expertise. We would like to thank the individuals involved in the making of this
thesis and the companies behind whom all made this Master‚Äôs thesis possible.
First of all our supervisor Associate Professor, PhD, Kurt Kielsgaard Hansen has been
an ever present and most helpful mentor and inexhaustible source of knowledge. His en-
thusiasm and persistence has been a constant source of motivation and we have enjoyed
working with him throughout our entire time at the Technical University of Denmark.
This Master‚Äôs thesis had five external supervisors representing the three branches of the
civil engineering industry: The cement production, the concrete mixing facilities, and
the contractors. We would like to thank BSc Ingrid Ahrenkilde, Manager of Research
and Quality Centre, and PhD Erik Pram Nielsen, Product Innovation and Commercial
Development Leader, both from Aalborg Portland A/S, for their expertise regarding the
technical and chemical processes of cements‚Äô setting and hardening process, their inter-
est in the investigations of self-compacting concrete‚Äôs setting time, and their initiative
to propose this project as a Master‚Äôs thesis.
MSc Ib B√¶lum Jensen, Technology Manager from Unicon A/S, has as external super-
visor been most helpful regarding the process of mixing and the use of self-compacting
concrete, and the most interesting aspects of the formwork pressure investigations in this
Master‚Äôs thesis. Without his commitment this project would not have had the extent it
has.
Representing the contractors MSc Srirangan Thangarajah, Concrete Specialist, and MSs
Anders Dahl Thomsen, Civil and Structual Engineer, both from Per Aarsleff A/S, has
as external supervisors been helpful regarding the execution of the full-scale experi-
ment regarding formwork pressure. Their experience with dimensioning formwork for
self-compacting concretes and their willingness to lent workforce and materials for the
experiment has made the full-scale experiment possible.
A special thanks goes to Unicon A/S which have provided the aggregates and additives
for mixing the vast amount of self-compacting concrete in DTU‚Äôs laboratory and pro-
viding the self-compacting concrete for the full-scale experiment. Furthermore, the staff

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Self-compacting concrete (abbreviated SCC) is a type of concrete that is under a still
growing usage worldwide. Especially in Denmark the use of SCC is widespread. Now
SCC is mostly used to make prefabricated concrete elements in factories or horizontal
in-situ castings, but only few in-situ walls are cast in SCC [VoSCC (2017)]. Because
of the unique ability of SCC to compact itself and thus no need of the additional work
phase of vibration, there is a huge potential for optimizing the entire work phase of
casting in-situ. Especially casting of walls can be sped up if it is possible to identify
the development of the formwork pressure and the setting time of the wall casting. The
suppliers of formwork only set the boundaries for formwork pressure and will not advise
with regards to rate of casting. Thus it is up to the contractor to design the formwork.
Due to inexperience with SCC most formwork in Denmark is still designed for hydro-
statical pressure or other conservative assumptions.
SCC and its attributes have been known for many years, but the complexity and vast
variation of different SCCs have proven difficult for scientists and practicians alike to
make an unambiguous practise for optimized vertical castings with SCC. For slabs and
pre-fabricated elements SCC has proven a big asset and is widely used. During the
last decade scientists from different countries have done various experiments regarding
casting methods of vertical castings such as walls and columns, and different equations
have been derived in order to calculate the formwork pressure from the fresh SCC. An
important factor is the setting time which the German standard DIN 18218 uses to cal-
culate the formwork pressure, and this standard is the recommended basis for formwork
pressure calculations of all larger formwork suppliers in Denmark.
This Master‚Äôs thesis aims to clarify the difference between the commonly used setting
time determination methods, and compare them to a specific experiment with measured
formwork pressure.
Regarding nomenclature this Master‚Äôs thesis refers to self-compacting concrete as SCC,
traditional concrete covers concrete that needs to be vibrated when cast, and concrete
is used for both (or all) types of concrete.
==PAGE:18==
2
1 Introduction
1.1
Thesis Statement
The objective of this Master‚Äôs thesis is threefold:
‚ÄĘ Firstly, the setting time of two commonly used SCCs for walls is determined using
four different methods in the laboratory.
Three commonly approved methods:
The Vicat Needle Test, the Proctor Penetration Test, and the Knead Bag Method.
Furthermore, one new method based on the heat development of the SCC will be
presented.
‚ÄĘ Secondly, the early strength development of the SCCs are determined by casting
cubes from the same batches of SCC from the laboratory experiments. By measur-
ing the temperature of the cubes, their maturity is calculated and the cubes are
strength tested at different times of maturity.
‚ÄĘ Thirdly, two walls are cast in a full-scale experiment using the most relevant of
the two SCCs and the formwork pressure is measured. Simultaneously to these
castings the setting time is determined using the same methods as in the laboratory
experiments and cubes are cast in order to compare the compressive strengths at
certain times of maturity.
With the results from the laboratory experiments it is striven to find a correlation
between the setting time and the formwork pressure of walls when using SCC.
1.2
Structure of the Thesis
This Master‚Äôs thesis begins with an introduction of SCC and a literature study on
some of the existing research on SCC and formwork pressure for walls. Thereafter a
chapter on the most important theory used in the experiments and the analysis of the
results is presented. The main part of the thesis is a description and presentation of
the experiments performed, both in the laboratory and in the full-scale experiment.
Lastly, the results are presented, discussed, and concluded. A complete and thorough
description of the setup, execution, and analysis of the experiments can be found in the
appendices. The attached USB flash drive contains a complete photo documentation of
all samples and experimental setups, raw data of all measurements, and spreadsheets
used in the analysis of all results.
1.3
Collaboration Partners
This Master‚Äôs thesis is done in collaboration with three major companies from the civil
engineering business in Denmark. With decades of experience from Denmark and abroad,
and representing each of the three branches of the concrete industry, supervisors from
these three companies has guided and advised the thesis work in the most relevant and
interesting direction. Representing the cement industry is Aalborg Portland A/S, the
==PAGE:19==
1.4 Self-Compacting Concrete
3
concrete factories are represented by Unicon A/S, and the contractors by Per Aarsleff
A/S. Furthermore, the Technological Institute of Denmark has been helpful regarding
the full-scale experiment concerning measuring the formwork pressure.
1.4
Self-Compacting Concrete
As the name self-compacting concrete implies the ability to self-compact is the main
difference between traditional concrete and SCC. The flow properties that define the
SCC can be described by the two factors shear yield stress and plastic viscosity [Pade,
Thrane & Nielsen (2008)]. Simplified these two parameters describe how far the SCC
can float and at which speed. A number of factors can be adjusted in order to design
the perfect SCC for a specific task e.g. a complicated casting with dense reinforcement,
many recesses, or a complicated design of the formwork. Adjustments should be done
wisely since too big flowability might result in segregation or other flaws of the SCC.
However mastering the design and casting method of the SCC will certainly improve the
time of casting and the money spent on manpower.
SCC was first used in Japan in the 1980s due to the invention of superplasticizers. At
that time it was widely accepted that the flowability of concrete was dependent on the
amount of mixed-in water. The invention of superplasticizers made it possible to divert
from this rule of thumb, and made it possible to produce easily workable concretes with-
out a high amount of water [VoSCC (2017)].
Since the 1980s a lot has happened with regards to SCC. The product and its use has
spread to the rest of the world mainly due to the further inventions of superplasticizers
that stabilizes the SCC in addition to making it more flowable. This stabilizing effect
helps to prevent separation why the casting process can be done without vibration.
Although the product has been on the marked for more than 35 years there is still a
big difference in how it has been received in different countries as well as how flowing it
should be. There is no specific definition of how flowing a concrete should be in order
to be categorized as a SCC, but a slump flow of over 500 mm is the rule of thumb in
Denmark, whereas it is larger in most other countries. The different definitions and
traditions in different countries make it difficult to make investigations on formwork
pressure of walls that are applicable internationally.
Despite the different definitions of SCC in different countries most agree that the flowa-
bility of SCC is measured with slump flow instead of normal slump as Figure 1.1 shows.
The unique properties that define SCC and the small amount of manual work needed
for casting are the main reasons most concrete companies and contractors regard SCC
as the future type of concrete.
==PAGE:20==
4
1 Introduction
Figure 1.1: The difference between traditional slump (left) and slump flow (right) for SCC.
Both methods is based on the standard slump cone. Picture from [VoSCC (2017)].
1.4.1
Potential and Use of SCC in Denmark
The use of SCC in Denmark has since its introduction in the start of the 21st century
gained a foothold in the Danish concrete industry in the element and in-situ production
[VoSCC (2017)]. Denmark is one of the leading countries in the world today, and by
far the leading country in Europe, regarding the part of produced SCC compared to
the total production of ready mixed concrete. Statistics from 2015 compiled by the
European Ready Mixed Concrete Organization (ERMCO) showed that 30% of the total
ready mixed concrete production in Denmark was made up by SCC, whereas the average
in Europe was 2.3%.
Although the Danish production of SCC is one of the highest in the world, it has been
stagnant for the last couple of years (30% in both 2013, 2014, and 2015). A potential
for an increased use of SCC is still feasible in certain fields of application. The use of
SCC in comparison to the total concrete production categorized into fields of application
processed by Dansk Byggeri and ERMCO can be seen in Figure 1.2.

==PAGE:21==
1.4 Self-Compacting Concrete
5
Figure 1.2 shows that the potential for use of SCC in the industry of prefabricated
concrete elements is nearly fulfilled. In other parts of the industry SCC is not used, but
the potential is likewise none-existing (lightweight pre-fabricated elements, hollow-core
slabs, etc.). The biggest potential lies within the ready mixed concrete industry where
it is assessed that SCC can be used in about 80% of all cases [VoSCC (2017)]. Of the
approximately 30% of the ready mixed concrete where SCC is used today, nearly all are
horizontal castings such as slaps, and nearly none vertical such as walls.
1.4.2
Use of SCC in Vertical Castings
Besides immediately economical, safety-wise, and working environmental advantages of
using SCC compared to traditional concrete in vertical castings, SCC can solve many
challenges regarding castings with high density reinforcement, complicated formwork
design, and inconvenient recesses in the formwork. In such constructions it is difficult
to vibrate the concrete properly. This is the reason why SCC is preferable in such situ-
ations since SCC is able to fulfill these requirements.
But not everything is favorable using SCC in vertical castings. Since the breakthrough of
SCC a number of challenges regarding mix design, flow abilities, and method of casting
have been addressed in order to minimize the risk of separation, blocking, and surface
finish. Meanwhile a major challenge is regarding the formwork. It has to be tighter
than for traditional concrete, and since SCC can be cast with significant higher rates
than the 1.0-1.5 m/h for traditional concrete, the formwork pressure gets much higher
than otherwise. This means the formwork for walls and columns usually is designed to
withhold a hydrostatical pressure due to the low viscosity of SCC and the faster rate
of casting. This is most of the time a very conservative design and the more expensive
formwork counterbalances the benefits of a faster casting.
Taking both advantages and disadvantages into account most still favors the use of SCC
in vertical castings. In 2007 MT H√łjgaard assessed the economical aspects of casting
walls of 4-5 meters height with SCC compared to traditional concrete [VoSCC (2017)].
The analysis was made with walls of 30-50 cm thickness under the following assumptions:
‚ÄĘ The formwork was designed to withhold a hydrostatical pressure,
‚ÄĘ The placement by pump was used with the same amount of man-hours. The price
of SCC was however at that time 10-20% higher than traditional concrete (today
it is only 6-13% higher cf. [DK-Beton (2017)] and [Unicon (2017)]).
‚ÄĘ Time used on vibrating traditional concrete, setting up and pulling down of vibra-
tion equipment, and rent of vibrating equipment could be saved by using SCC.
The savings by using SCC instead of traditional concrete, with the above mentioned
assumptions, was 135 DKK/m3 concrete. For walls under 3 meters height the savings
would be even larger. This is due to the extra reinforcement of the formwork required
==PAGE:22==
6
1 Introduction
for tall castings (high hydrostatical pressure). In other words, if the formwork can be
dimensioned to withhold less than hydrostatical pressure the savings will be higher for
high vertical castings.
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 2
Literature Study
This chapter is a short study of SCC literature.
Main sources of knowledge is the
Danish SCC-Consortium who researched in SCC for a three year period and published a
number of papers and handbooks regarding both design and execution of SCC castings.
Another source is the 3rd International Symposium on Self-Compacting Concrete from
which three articles regarding formwork pressure on walls have been investigated, and
finally the German standard DIN 18218 and other non-affiliated articles such as MSc
L. F. Kristensen‚Äôs Development of New Method for Determination of Concrete Setting
Time.
2.1
The SCC-Consortium
The Innovation Consortium for Self-Compacting Concrete, abbreviated The SCC-Con-
sortium, was a consortium existing between 2003-2007 with the scope to bring together
research, development, and innovation on the SCC field in Denmark. The aim was to
make SCC the most used concrete in Denmark before 2008. The consortium consisted
of leading Danish companies in civil engineering along with national as well as inter-
national knowledge centers. The most known of the members of the consortium was
4K Beton A/S, back then Denmark‚Äôs leading manufacturer of ready-mix concrete and
now a part of Unicon A/S, MT H√łjgaard A/S, Denmark‚Äôs largest industrial contractor,
Aalborg Portland A/S, the worlds largest producer of white cement, the Department of
Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark, and the Danish Technological
Institute. The SCC-Consortium produced a number of papers and reports of which the
most relevant are summarized in the following sections.
2.1.1
Preliminary Investigations
Prior to the creation of the SCC-Consortium, the Danish Technological Institute pub-
lished a report in 2001 on the use of SCC in walls and columns [Glavind & Frederiksen
(2001)]. The scope of the experiments was to investigate the formwork pressure of walls
or columns over 2-3 m height. In cooperation with the formwork supplier Paschal A/S
two walls of 5 m and 6 m respectively were cast. The SCC was supplied by Unicon A/S
and consisted of their Vibreton¬© concrete with a slump flow of 600 mm, a density of
2250 kg/m3, and a temperature of 16¬įC.
==PAGE:24==

formwork pressure almost equal to hydrostatical pressure.1

2.1.2
Guidelines for Mix Design of SCC
The SCC Consortium published a handbook called ‚ÄĚGuidelines for Mix Design of SCC‚ÄĚ
in which the basic properties of SCC is described along with the most relevant correlation
between concrete composition and rheology explained [Pade, Thrane & Nielsen (2008)
(1)]. This literature shall be seen as a background knowledge of the parameters in charge
of designing a SCC even though this Master‚Äôs thesis does not deal with the actual design
of SCC.
2.1.2.1
Rheological parameters of SCC
As a natural consequence of SCC‚Äôs ability to compact itself, and thus not need to be
vibrated, the biggest deviation between traditional concrete and SCC is the consistence.
SCC‚Äôs flow properties is characterized by the combination of two parameters:
‚ÄĘ The yield stress ŌĄ0 describes the SCC‚Äôs ability to float, i.e. how far it can float.
The yield stress is closely related to the slump flow. A high yield stress equals a
low slump flow and vice versa.
‚ÄĘ The plastic viscosity ő∑pl describes the workability of the SCC. A high viscosity
means the SCC is thick and sticky and thus difficult to spread out. A low viscosity
on the other hand means the SCC is easily workable and floats quickly.
A material whose flow ability is described by a yield stress and a plastic viscosity is
called a Bingham material. The consequence of adjusting either the yield stress or the
plastic viscosity will be perceived according to Figure 2.3 by the user (i.e. the concrete
worker).
==PAGE:26==
10
2 Literature Study
Figure 2.3: Consequences of adjusting the rheological parameters of SCC as perceived by the
concrete worker, cf. [Pade, Thrane & Nielsen (2008) (1)].
The rheological parameters can be measured by a slump flow test and by the use of a
viscometer.
2.1.2.2
Composition and Consequence of Adjustments in Design
A SCC design can consist of the following parameters:
‚ÄĘ Amount of aggregate (ŌÜ),
‚ÄĘ Aggregate shape (f/k),
‚ÄĘ Fine aggregate grain curve,
‚ÄĘ Water to cement ratio,
‚ÄĘ Fly ash,
‚ÄĘ Silica fume,
‚ÄĘ Superplasticizing additive (SP),
‚ÄĘ Viscosity modifying agents (VMA),
‚ÄĘ Air entrainment.
Adjusting one or more of the parameters will affect the rheology of the SCC. Table 2.1
summarizes the consequences of adjusting the mix design.
==PAGE:27==

The handbook does not provide any conclusion but rather a resume of the parameters
in charge of the final SCC product and their effect on the final SCC, see above table.
Therefore the handbook provides a good basic knowledge of SCC relevant for anyone
working with SCC.
2.1.3
Guidelines for Execution of SCC
The SCC Consortium published a handbook called ‚ÄĚGuidelines for Execution of SCC‚ÄĚ
in which knowledge about how the execution of SCC should differ from execution of
traditional concrete [Pade, Thrane & Nielsen (2008) (2)]. The purpose of the handbook
was to contribute to a better understanding regarding the use of SCC in order to avoid
pitfalls and minimize the risk of insufficient quality when using SCC in construction
work. This literature shall be seen as a background knowledge of SCC execution used in
order for the planning and execution of the full-scale experiment in this Master‚Äôs thesis.
2.1.3.1
SCC Compared to Traditional Concrete
SCC is defined as a type of concrete that flows by itself into the formwork and encloses
the reinforcement without using vibration or other kinds of external mechanical impact.
It is however allowed to affect the SCC manually with rake and shovel, and by utilizing
the gravitational energy e.g.
by dropping the concrete from the pump hose.
Using
SCC will in this way omit a lot of work processes which entails increased productivity
and an improved working environment. While properties such as strength, rigidity, and
durability are almost similar for SCC as suppose to ordinary concrete, the consistence is
seen to be completely different. The consistence (more fluid than ordinary concrete) of
SCC makes major demands for the formwork and the form filling as well as an increased
awareness is needed after filling to avoid segregation, blocking, and surface finish.
2.1.3.2
Formwork
No further demands for the execution of formwork is needed when using SCC. Due to
the high flowability of SCC certain differences must however be taken into account when
==PAGE:28==
12
2 Literature Study
choosing and erecting the formwork:
‚ÄĘ The formwork shall be designed to resist hydrostatical pressure: Some pressure
measurements have revealed that the high flowability of SCC have an impact on
the time dependant development of the formwork pressure, from the initial state
where the concrete behaves like a liquid, until the setting begins.
The initial
build-up of the internal strength causes the formwork pressure to not reach the
hydrostatical level and is thereby effected to a certain extent of the casting rate.
For SCC this effect will however only occur for fairly low casting rates which is
typically not relevant since breaks in the casting of SCC are not allowed.
‚ÄĘ Increased requirements for the tightness: A leaking formwork will lead to seepage
of cement paste and small cracks (few millimeters) will cause a risk of honey
combing2
‚ÄĘ Embedded recess and hollow boxes needs to be secured against floating due to the
hydrostatical pressure from the SCC.
2.1.3.3
Form Filling
Placing SCC in the formwork is recommended to be done in one of the three ways as
shown in figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Three traditional methods for placing SCC. From left: Conveyor belt, pumping
from top of the formwork, and pumping through an inlet joint from the bottom of the formwork.
From [Pade, Thrane & Nielsen (2008) (2)] edited by authors.
The three casting methods are shortly described:
1. Casting from a conveyor belt: This method requires that the SCC is not too
fluent and that the belt angle does not deviate significantly from a horizontal angle
as illustrated in Figure 2.4 (left).
2. Pumping SCC from above the formwork: This method is the most commonly
used especially for horizontal castings such as slabs.
When casting SCC into
vertical walls a hanging pump hose is needed to cast between reinforcement as
2Stenreder in Danish.
==PAGE:29==
2.1 The SCC-Consortium
13
illustrated in Figure 2.4 (middle). Using this method on vertical walls strongly
depends on the thickness of the wall and the risk of congestion due to the density
of the reinforcement.
3. Pumping SCC from the bottom of the formwork through a pump inlet connec-
tion as illustrated in Figure 2.4 (right): This method is mostly applied to vertical
castings with a very difficult geometry and a high risk of congestion due to the
reinforcement design.
Regardless of the casting method, the drop height of the SCC must never exceed 1 meter.
This is why placing of SCC using a crane bucket is not recommended since this method
typically means increased fall heights, uneven casting rates, and a difficult control of the
formwork filling. When pumping is used as the casting method an increased focus must
be paid to the risk of separation and blocking of the pump, which is why pump pressure
is important to control.
2.1.4
Filling Formwork with SCC, DR Byen
As part of the work of the SCC-Consortium an investigation on the usage of SCC for
walls was tested at the construction of the Concert Centre DR Byen (home of the Danish
Broadcasting Company) in 2007 [Thrane (2007)]. Two different types of SCC were used
to cast a total amount of five walls with a similar geometry of 400 x 500 x 50 cm and
similar reinforcement with an amount of 88 kg per m3. A sixth and more complicated
core wall was also cast. The purpose of the investigation was to examine the fresh and
hardening properties of SCC as well as observe its behaviour under use of different casting
methods and map the correlation in proportion to blocking and separation. Lastly the
formwork pressure was investigated to test if SCC creates a hydrostatical pressure.
2.1.4.1
Experimental Setup
The five walls were of the dimensions 400 x 500 x 50 cm and were reinforced with
Y16/150 in both direction and in front and back and U-bars Y12/300 in casting joints
in top and bottom corresponding to an amount of 88 kg reinforcement per m3. Two
different types of SCC were used:
‚ÄĘ Type E40: Extra aggressive environmental class, characteristic strength 40 MPa,
and equivalent w/c-ratio of maximum 0.40.
‚ÄĘ Type M30: Moderate environmental class, characteristic strength 30 MPa, and
equivalent w/c-ratio of maximum 0.55.
33 pressure measure devices were attached to each wall in order to plot the pressure when
filling the forms with SCC. The five identical walls was filled using different methods
shown in Table 2.2.
==PAGE:30==

2.1.4.2
Filling of Formwork
It is the experience of the Consortium that it is possible to fill most straight walls with
SCC within a wide range of rheological properties. It might be necessary to move the
pump hose in the end in order to prevent a sloping top. Are the walls of more complicated
design, or are the reinforcement dense or complicated, the rheological properties has to
be taken more into account. A low yield stress might be required in order to let the
SCC flow out into every corner of a form, but if the SCC has to flow too far from the
pump hose the risk of separation is highly increased. Complicated form geometry and
reinforcement design does not prevent a quick casting of up to 25 m/h. Taking into
account the desire for a neat surface finish and regarding the formwork pressure, such a
high casting speed might not be possible.
==PAGE:31==
2.1 The SCC-Consortium
15
2.1.4.3
Formwork Pressure
The conclusions regarding formwork pressure is that hydrostatical pressure has to be
assumed when casting with the high speeds used in these experiments (observed for
18 m/h speeds). This is regardless of rheological parameters. It is presumed that the
rheological parameters and casting technique plays a bigger part when casting at slower
speeds (under 2 m/h). The authors refer in this case to [Billberg (2003)], see section
2.2.1.
2.1.5
Self-Compacting Concrete - SCC
In 2004 the SCC Consortium published an overall article on SCC in the Danish magazine
‚ÄĚBeton-Teknik‚ÄĚ written by J. Thrys√łe and T. Rasmussen from Aalborg Portland, and by
A. Berrig, M. T. Hasholt, and C. Pade from the Danish Technological Institute [Thrys√łe,
Rasmussen, Berrig, Hasholt, & Pade (2004)]. The article covers most aspects of SCC
and its use, but only slightly touches the expected formwork pressure on vertical castings.
The best results regarding the surface of the done wall is achieved by casting with the
pump submerged a bit under the surface of the SCC or by pumping from the bottom.
Allowing the SCC to fall may result in separation or inhomogeneous air distribution.
The formwork should be designed to withstand at least hydrostatical pressure when
pumping from the bottom - the report only concludes that when filling the formwork
from the top, casting speed, viscosity, and thrixotropy affects the formwork pressure.
Figure 2.6 shows the results of the different experiments conducted in the report. The
experiments are not described any further than this figure.
Figure 2.6: Formwork pressure is dependent on casting method and casting speed but also rate
of reinforcement and geometry. From [Thrys√łe, Rasmussen, Berrig, Hasholt, & Pade (2004)].
==PAGE:32==
16
2 Literature Study
2.2
3rd International Symposium on Self-Compacting
Concrete
Three articles from the 3rd International Symposium on Self-Compacting Concrete of
August 2003 deal with formwork pressure from SCC on walls. The three articles mainly
regard whether or not hydrostatical pressure can be assumed.
2.2.1
Form Pressure Generated by Self-Compacting Concrete
Peter Billberg of the Swedish Cement and Concrete Research Institute, CBI, presents in
this paper the experiences gained by a project aiming at finding the parameters control-
ling the thixotropical properties of self-compacting bridge concrete performed between
1999 and 2002 [Billberg (2003)].
Previous reports have indicated that casting walls
with SCC would cause an increased formwork pressure compared to traditional vibrated
concrete. Several experiments with bridges cast with SCC in Sweden have proved the
opposite though. Using different mix designs as well as different casting rates the form-
work pressure was measured.
With two different mix designs and casting rates between 0.8 and 2.3 m/h seven walls
of 3.0 x 3.5 x 0.3 m were cast. As reference one similar wall was cast with a traditional
vibrated concrete. All walls were cast by pumping from the top of the formwork with
a drop of 1¬Ī0.5 m. The two mix designs had slump flows of 730 mm and 700 mm
respectively with a tolerance of ¬Ī50 mm.
The conclusions of the report were the following:
‚ÄĘ A higher casting speed equals a higher formwork pressure,
‚ÄĘ The younger a SCC (and thus higher slump flow) the higher the formwork pressure,
‚ÄĘ An even higher casting rate will lead to a hydrostatical pressure on the formwork,
‚ÄĘ While the SCC is handled (i.e.
mixed, transported, pumped, and placed) the
pressure is hydrostatical, but as soon as the SCC is still, the pressure decreases,
‚ÄĘ Variation of mix design cannot be assigned a specific influence on the formwork
pressure.
The author highlights the danger of casting whole walls too fast and thus achieving
hydrostatical pressure which might cause form collapse.
==PAGE:33==
2.2 3rd International Symposium on Self-Compacting Concrete
17
2.2.2
Investigations on the Formwork Pressure Using
Self-Compacting Concrete
Wolfgang Brameshuber and Stephan Uebachs of the Institute of Building Materials Re-
search (IBAC) of Aachen University investigates the formwork pressure of five different
walls all measuring 3.51 x 0.24 x 3.30 m [Brameshuber & Uebachs (2003)]. Using the
‚ÄĚDr√∂ssler Easyflow‚ÄĚ SCC (which is a widely used SCC in Germany) they looked at two
different casting rates (10 m/h and 2 m/h) either pumped from the bottom of the form-
work, or dropped from a bucket from above. The SCC contained fly ash, had an average
slump flow of 700-750 mm, and a fresh bulk density of 2350 kg/m3. The setting time
were approximately 8 hours at temperatures of about 15-18¬įC. As a reference one of
the walls were cast with a traditional (vibrated) concrete. Table 2.3 shows how the five
walls were cast.

2 Literature Study
The figure clearly shows that pumping from the bottom results in a hydrostatical pres-

Filling the forms with a bucket from above resulted in a much lower pressure on the
formwork (see Figure 2.7) and the slower rate of casting reduced the pressure even fur-
ther.
Brameshuber and Uebachs conclude that the casting method first of all influences the
formwork pressure greatly: Pumping from the bottom of the formwork doubles the
formwork pressure and makes it hydrostatical (nearly) regardless of the casting rate.
Decreasing the casting rate decreases the formwork pressure when filling from the top.
The German standard DIN 18218 can be used when calculating formwork pressure when
pumping from the bottom. The influence of rheology and temperature was not clarified
in the report but Brameshuber & Uebachs pointed out that they thought it relevant to
do so.

Five sensors were placed in the formwork in order to measure the formwork pressure
along the height of the wall. The lowest were placed 150 mm above the ground and the
remaining four with 600 mm distance.
The forms in the laboratory were filled with two batches of approximately 200 liters
SCC with a break of about 20 minutes between the batches. The traditional concrete
was dumped directly into the form, and the SCC via a channel of 35¬į slope. It took 1.5
minutes to fill one batch into the form.
In the field study the SCC was pumped into the form through a valve 600 mm above the
bottom after 1 hour of transportation. The SCC was pumped with a constant pressure
of 1.5-2.0 MPa. The form was filled in 15 minutes corresponding to a rate of casting of
18.8 m/h.
Figure 2.9 (a) shows the recorded formwork pressure of the laboratory experiments.
The graph shows the pressure as percentage of hydrostatical pressure. SCC 1-3 ranges
between 87% and 90% of the hydrostatical pressure at the height of the lowest sensor.
The pressure starts to decrease immediately after casting. After about 2 hours all SCCs
except ‚ÄĚSCC 1‚ÄĚ has decreased below 50% of the maximum pressure, see figure 2.9 (b).
After two hours the conventional concrete shows a faster decrease of pressure than the
SCCs.

2.3
Articles based on DIN 18218
The German standard DIN 18218:2010 ‚ÄĚPressure of Fresh Concrete on Vertical Form-
work‚ÄĚ was updated in 2010 to include formwork pressure from SCC. An article from Con-
crete International (June 2010) summarizes the changes [Concrete International (2010)].
2.3.1
DIN Standard on Formwork Pressure Updated
Technische Universit√§t Darmstadt and other German research institutes made a series
of investigations in order to update the German Standard DIN 18218 ‚ÄúPressure of Fresh
Concrete on Vertical Formwork‚ÄĚ in regards to the use of SCC in vertical castings. The
two biggest factors affecting the formwork pressure from SCC on vertical castings was
found to be setting time and placement rate. More specifically the maximum pressure
was found to increase with an increasing water-cementitious material ratio (w/cm), due
to the correlation between w/cm and setting time3. The pressure was also significantly
influenced by the type of high-range water-reducing admixture, the type and volume of
fly ash, and the cement type, since this had an effect on the setting time as well.
The standard was updated to define a characteristic nominal pressure ŌÉhk,max in [kPa]
for SCC as
ŌÉhk,max = (1.0 meter + 0.26őĹ ¬∑ tE) ¬∑ ő≥C ‚Č•30 kPa
where ŌÉhk,max represents the 95th percentile value of the maximum pressure exerted by
the fresh concrete, ő≥C is the unit weight of fresh concrete, tE is the setting time of the
concrete (using the Vicat Needle Test), and őĹ is the mean rate of concrete placement.
The equation is only valid for tE from 5 to 20 hours.
Figure 2.11 illustrates how the concrete pressure increases hydrostatically from the con-
crete surface to the height hS where ŌÉhk,max = hS ¬∑ ő≥C. For H greater than hE = őĹ ¬∑ tE
the pressure exerted by the concrete decreases to zero. ő≥F is a partial safety factor and
ŌÉhdmax the corresponding design value for the pressure.
3Note that w/cm-ratio is the same as the previously mentioned WB-ratio but different countries
have different ways of notation. As this is a literature study, the notations used in the original texts
are preserved.
==PAGE:38==
22
2 Literature Study
Figure 2.11: Lateral pressure distribution of SCC per DIN 18218:2010. Figure from [Concrete
International (2010)].
A correlation between casting rate and maximum formwork pressure was also visualized
with different consistency classes plotted. Not surprisingly the more fluent a concrete
the higher pressure could be expected, and the higher rate of casting the higher the
pressure as well.
2.4
Other Relevant Articles
2.4.1
Correlation Between Heat Development and Proctor
Penetration Test
MSc L. F. Kristensen has in his article Development of New Method for Determination
of Concrete Setting Time [Kristensen (2011)] shown a correlation between heat devel-
opment in concrete and the penetration resistance to be 0.99. Inspired by the growing
market of SCC in Denmark and the prolonged setting time in SCC compared to tradi-
tional slump concrete, and the lack of a semi-automatic measuring method to determine
the setting time of concrete, he has proven a nearly exact correlation between heat de-
velopment and setting time (defined by the Proctor Penetration Test).
Kristensen‚Äôs experiment starts by sieving concrete through a 8 mm mesh sieve. The
mortar is collected into a 0.1 liter cylinder. A thermo thread is cast into the middle of
the sample and the sample is put into an insulated container. The temperature is then
==PAGE:39==
2.4 Other Relevant Articles
23
logged via a PC. Simultaneously the Proctor Penetration Test is carried out. Briefly
explained it consists of penetrating different size plungers into the mortar (also sieved
through a 8 mm mesh sieve) and registering the applied force with which to penetrate
the mortar. The force divided by the area of the plunger equals the strength of the
mortar. When 3.5 MPa strength is achieved the mortar has reached initial setting. The
heat development measurements can then be compared to the strength development.
Figure 2.12 shows the schematic setup of the experiment.
Figure 2.12: Schematically illustration of heat development measurement, from [Kristensen
(2011)].
Eight different common Danish concretes (including two SCCs) have been tested. As
Table 2.5 shows, different setting times, cement types, and workability (ranging from
slump 120 mm to slump flow 550) have been tested. Some of the mix designs have been
performed several times why the total number of tests is 18.

compared to proctor penetration. He shows that when the heat development reaches
a certain point it corresponds to the initial setting time using the Proctor Penetration
Test, regardless of type of cement, traditional concrete or SCC, and different strength
classes.

CHAPTER 3
Theory
This chapter summarizes and explains the most relevant theory for this Master‚Äôs thesis.
The theory is presented in the order it occurs during casting, setting, and finally the
testing of the hardened SCC. Following additional theory can be found in Appendix A:
‚ÄĘ Determination of moisture contents in aggregates,
‚ÄĘ Adjustments of water in SCC mix design,
‚ÄĘ Setting time, and
‚ÄĘ Calculation of compressive strength.
3.1
Heat Development and Maturity in Concrete
An important part of this Master‚Äôs thesis is to investigate the correlation between the
temperature development in fresh (hardening) SCC and the corresponding strength. It
is thus important to clarify the definitions of maturity and how it develops according to
the heat development in the freshly mixed SCC.
3.1.1
Heat Development
When cement mixes with water a chemical process initiates which develops heat. This
process is called hydration. The four main components in Portland-cement are the clinker
minerals C3S, C2S, C3A, and C4AF. When complete hydration occurs the clinker min-
erals develop a heat amount of approximately 500, 260, 900, and 300 kJ/kg respectively
cf. [S√łrensen (1981)]1. In Aalborg Portland‚Äôs RAPID¬ģ cement the distribution of the
clinker minerals are as listed in Table 3.1.
Table 3.1: Distribution of clinker minerals in Aalborg Portland‚Äôs RAPID¬ģ cement cf. [Cement
& Beton (2016)]
Content in [%]
C3S
C2S
C3A
C4AF
RAPID¬ģ cement
53
20
8
12
1According to [Beton-Bogen (1985)] p. 359 the heat developed is 500, 260, 870, and 420 J/g for
C3S, C2S, C3A, and C4AF respectively.
==PAGE:42==
26
3 Theory
Due to the large amount of C3S RAPID¬ģ cement has a quick and high heat development
compared to other regular cement types. The heat development from the hydration can
be measured in different ways where these are the three most common cf. [S√łrensen
(1981)]:
1. Calorimetry by Dissolution where the dissolution heat in acid is determined
by a series of tests of cement paste of different hydration times.
2. Calorimetry by Conductivity where the flow of heat from a small specimen of
cement paste which hydrates under a constant temperature is measured.
3. Adiabatic Calorimetry where the specimen hardens under adiabatic conditions,
i.e.
where the specimen does not exchange heat with the surroundings.
Thus
is the hydration heat completely transformed into a temperature increase in the
specimen.
The most common method used in Denmark is adiabatic calorimetry or variants inspired
by it (known as semi-adiabetic calorimetry) [Beton-Teknik (1989)]. Ideally the specimen
is completely isolated from the surroundings either by adjusting the surrounding tem-
perature according to the specimen or to imitate this practically difficult criteria, by
isolating it in a box. This kind of test is commonly known as the Hay Box Test and is
described in two Nordtest Methods: NT BUILD 388 and NT BUILD 480. The heat de-
velopment, Q, can be expressed as energy pr. kg cement and determined by the following
equations [NT Build 388]:
‚ąÜQn = Qacc,n + ‚ąÜQtrans,n =

3.1.2
Maturity Calculations
The temperature of a concrete has great influence on the speed with which the hydra-
tion happens and thus the speed with which the properties of the concrete develops cf.
[Kielsgaard Hansen (2008)] p. 61. Hardened concrete achieves its designed strength
after 28 days of maturity why this maturity is decisive for when a concrete can be tested
for e.g. compressive strength. Maturity is the equivalent hardening time at 20¬įC in an
environment where the hardening concrete has the appropriate amount of water in order
for the hydration to happen. The maturity is defined by:

3.2
Nomenclature Regarding Setting
Stiffening is the loss of consistence by the plastic cement paste, whereas setting refers
to the solidification, i.e. initial setting occurs when the paste/SCC becomes unworkable,
==PAGE:45==
3.3 Compressive Strength Comparison
29
and final setting when the paste/SCC is fully rigid (complete solidification). After the
final setting the strength begins to develop more rapidly and the hardening of the SCC
begins.
These definitions are according to [Mehta & Monterio (2014)] p.
211, but
different norms, standards, and methods might differ how they define initial and final
setting.
3.3

Strength classes such as C35 refers to a cylindrical specimen‚Äôs compressive strength
of minimum 35 MPa. DS/EN 206:2013 Table 12 lists the corresponding compressive
strength of cubes compared to cylinders. To avoid confusion this Master‚Äôs thesis converts
all results from compressive strength tests of 150 mm x 150 mm x 150 mm cubes to
corresponding 300 mm high √ł150 mm cylinders using the conversion factors listed below
in Table 3.3 based on Table 12 in DS/EN 206:2013. The conversion factor is simply
derived by dividing the cylinder strength with the cube strength.

3.4
Calculation of Formwork Pressure
The calculation of formwork pressure might seem simple, and not a topic of dispute,
but only the very conservative method of assuming concrete as a pure fluid, and thus
adapt a pressure distribution corresponding to a hydrostatical pressure, is commonly
accepted in various countries. Different norms and standards offer other methods with
which to calculate a more precise formwork pressure, but these differ from country to
country and between the formwork suppliers. Figure 3.2 shows a wall filled with concrete.
The formwork pressure distribution shows the maximum formwork pressure, Pmax, when
filling the formwork, and the total distribution shows the amount of pressure acting on
the formwork throughout the whole casting process.
==PAGE:46==
30
3 Theory
Figure 3.2: Schematic representation of a newly cast wall and its formwork pressure distri-
bution. The lower part of the wall (B-C) is all affected by the maximum pressure at some
point during casting, whereas the upper part (A-B) is not. Thus is the lower part of the wall‚Äôs
formwork more reinforced than the upper. Figure from [Anl√¶gsteknikforeningen (2011)] p. 464
edited to English by this Master‚Äôs thesis authors.
Even though the setting time, and thus hardening, of the concrete is highly dependent
on temperature, the maximum formwork pressure, Pmax, depends on a number of factors
[Anl√¶gsteknikforeningen (2011)] p. 463-468:
‚ÄĘ Rate of casting: The maximal pressure increases with increased casting rate
because hmax (distance to self-bearing concrete) increases.
‚ÄĘ The height of the formwork: The highest pressure occurs when formwork is
filled in one load (columns and thin walls) and before setting occurs. In the bottom
of the formwork the pressure will correspond to the weight of the concrete in the
full height (hform).
‚ÄĘ The consistence of the concrete: A lower slump results in lower pressure.
‚ÄĘ The density of the concrete: Dependent on aggregates the density of the
concrete varies.
‚ÄĘ The casting temperature of the concrete: A higher temperature results in
a lower formwork pressure, especially with slow casting rates, since the setting
occurs more rapidly than with a lower temperature.
‚ÄĘ Ambient temperature: The ambient temperature affects the concrete and thus
the rate with which the setting occurs. During winter it might be necessary to
heat or insulate the formwork.
‚ÄĘ The roughness and tightness of the formwork: A smoother and very tight
surface results in a higher formwork pressure due to the less friction between con-
crete and formwork.
==PAGE:47==
3.4 Calculation of Formwork Pressure
31
‚ÄĘ Vibration: When concrete is vibrated (not for SCCs) all internal friction is elim-
inated and a full (hydrostatical) pressure occurs.
‚ÄĘ Type of cement: Slowly setting cement or the use of retarder or pozzolans will
increase the formwork pressure.
‚ÄĘ The width of the construction: A larger form is filled more slowly and thus
decreases the formwork pressure.
Since no formula incorporates all of the above factors, a number of different methods
are used in different countries. Three of the biggest formwork suppliers who operate
in Denmark recommend the methods listed in Table 3.4 when calculating the formwork
pressure.2

==PAGE:50==

3 Theory
‚ÄĘ If the density of the fresh concrete ő≥C deviates from the value 25 kN/m3 (equal
to 2545 kg/m3), the fresh concrete pressure determined must be multiplied by a
factor K2 = ő≥C/25 (ő≥C in kN/m3).
‚ÄĘ The influence of the concrete temperature may be taken into account, since this
affect both the setting time and the rheological behavior and hence its pressure.
The fresh concrete pressure may be:
‚Äď reduced by 3% for every 1 K the placing temperature of the concrete in the
formwork is higher than the reference temperature used to determine the end
of setting (20¬įC),
‚Äď increased with 5% for every 1 K the placing temperature of the concrete is
lower than the reference temperature.
The difference between those temperatures may never exceed more than 5 K.
‚ÄĘ The SCC may not contain aggregates with a size bigger than 63 mm.
‚ÄĘ The formwork may not deviate from vertical direction by more than ¬Ī 5¬į.
‚ÄĘ The concrete is placed in opposite direction to the rise in level (placed from the
top).
‚ÄĘ No reliable estimation of the final setting can be made for concrete with temper-
atures lower than 15¬įC or where retarding admixtures are used. NB: Addition of
superplasticizers (used in most SCCs) can considerably retard the setting process
witch means experiments is needed to determine the setting time.
‚ÄĘ Reinforced building parts will reduce the fresh concrete pressure significantly, e.g.
a building part not wider than 0.5 m with a horizontal and vertical bar mesh
not more than 125 mm with bar diameter ‚Č•8 mm means reduced fresh concrete
pressure of 20%.
3.4.5

Hydrostatical pressure is when the concrete is assumed to work as a fluid and thus
behave like water. The maximum formwork pressure will be the density of the concrete
multiplied by the height of the concrete.

CHAPTER 4
SCC Recipes
In this Master‚Äôs thesis two SCC recipes are investigated. The recipes chosen to be ex-
amined are Unicon‚Äôs two most used SCCs for walls. The first, called A35, is a SCC used
in aggressive environmental class with a characteristic compressive strength of 35 MPa.
It is usually used for walls which needs to be moisture tight. The other, called M30, is a
SCC used in a moderate environmental class with a characteristic compressive strength
30 MPa usually used for watertight walls.
This chapter consists of the following sections:
‚ÄĘ Specifications, properties, and mix design for the two used SCCs,
‚ÄĘ Adjustments done relative to the original recipes from Unicon (which can be found
in Appendix B),
‚ÄĘ Information on plasticizer as an additive.
Appendix C further describes the individual parts that make up the SCCs.

4.2
Final Mix Design
Some adjustments in proportion to the original mix designs were needed in order to
make the recipes fit for use.
The only supplier of fly ash that qualifies for the Danish standard DS/EN 450-1, an-
nounced prior to the execution of the experiments that fly ash of the type B4 was no
longer on the market due to the coal-fired power plant‚Äôs green conversion plan. Alter-
natively a new type of fly ash called EmiFlyveAske was used. The new type of fly ash
has the same density (2300 kg/m3) and is hailed from the same power plants as the B4.
The EmiFlyveaske is however categorized as A/N, and is therefore allowed to have a loss
of ignition LOI ‚Č§5.0% (B4 had ‚Č§4.0%) which indicates the amount of unburned carbon.
An increased amount of unburned carbon (LOI) corresponds according to [Aalborg Port-
land (2017)] to:
‚ÄĘ Difficulties with sufficient air in the concrete, probably because the coal with its
great fineness acts as ‚ÄĚactivated charcoal‚ÄĚ and absorbs and inactivates the air
entertainment agent.
The need for air entertainment (additives) will therefore
increase relative to the normal dosage.
‚ÄĘ Higher w/cm-ratio to achieve the same workability. This means changes in w/cm-
ratio or the amount of additives might be necessary to get the right flowability of
the concrete.
The use of EmiFlyveAske instead of B4 is assumed not to lead to larger variation or de-
viation with regards to initial and final setting than would occur anyway because of the
variation of the materials. This assumption is backed by this Master‚Äôs thesis supervisor
from Unicon A/S.
A gap in the volume occurs when converting industrial waste water to tap water. The
industrial waste water is filtered and rinsed water from cleaning the concrete mixing
transport trucks, which is why it has a solid mass constitute of very fine grains like sand.
The industrial waste water have a density and solid mass content in accordance with
DS/EN 1008:2002. Even though this gap in volume should be solved by adjusting all the
aggregates to obtain the same proportion and grain curve it is chosen to close this gap
in volume only by adding sand. This is decided based on guidance from the supervisors
==PAGE:54==
38
4 SCC Recipes
from Unicon and Aalborg Portland.
The finial mix designs will, as a result of these issues and solutions, be as listed in Table
4.4 and Table 4.5. Both tables show the original mix design (left) and the final mix

4.3 SCC Components

4.3
SCC Components
The following section describes the additive plasticizer and its influence on the SCC. All
other SCC components can be found in Appendix C.
4.3.0.1
Plasticizer
Plasticizers can be divided into two different types: Regular plasticizers, also called
Water-Reducing Admixtures (WRA), and superplasticizers, also called High-Range Wa-
ter Reducers (HRWR). Both types work plasticizing as the substances appear disper-
sive ‚ÄĚlubricating‚ÄĚ thereby creating a workable/liquid concrete. However the plasticzers
(WRA) make less cement particles stick together while superplasticizers makes the ce-
ment grains totally separated as shown in Figure 4.1. The combination of good work-
ability and complete separation of the grains when superplasticizer is used entail the
perfect condition for a better and faster hydration of each cement grain, but at the same
time a opportunity to reduce the w/cm-ratio to obtain a concrete with better strength
but without changing its consistence.
Figure 4.1: The separation influence on the the cement grains in concrete when adding either
plasticizer (WRA) or superplasticizer (HRWR). Figure edited by authors.
Originally from
course CVEN9824 Advanced Materials Technology from University of New South Wales.
Superplasticizers (HRWR) are mainly based on water-soluble modified polycarboxylate
polymers. The modified polycarboxylate will adsorb to the cement grains after which
each grain will repel from each other due to steric hindrance that happens when the
adsorbed polycarboxylate molecules starts to overlap each other as illustrated in Figure
4.2.
The superplasticizer used in this Master‚Äôs thesis is of the type MasterGlenium
SKY 631.
It is a slight acid yellowish dissolution with a characteristic odor, which
is not compatible with other superplasticizers. It has to be mixed into the concrete
after the water has been added but only in a dosage of 0.2-2.0 weight-% of cement +
Supplementary Cementitious Material (SCM).
==PAGE:56==
40
4 SCC Recipes
Figure 4.2: Illustration of how platicizers work. Left: A principle sketch and a closeup of a
concrete where no plasticizer is used and therefore the cement particles are sticking to each other
while water is trapped. Right: A principle sketch and a closeup of a concrete with plasticizer.
Here cement grains are separated and entrapped water released because of the repulsion between
the grains due to the absorbed plasticizers. Figure edited by authors. Originally from course
CVEN9824 Advanced Materials Technology from University of New South Wales.
Plasticizers (WRA) are based on lignosulfonates. Lignosulfonates work similar to the
polycarboxylate, but are however slightly retarding meaning that each grain will be
coated and therefore disallowed all contact with water and thus avoiding them to hydrate.
A slightly retarding effect in SCC entails a delaying in the setting time why it meets
the concrete plant‚Äôs desire of a slow reduction in the consistence and therefore a longer
time span in the transport to the construction sites. The plasicizer used in this Master‚Äôs
thesis is of the type MasterPozzlith 20N. It is a slight acid dark brown dissolution with
a characteristics odor, that can be added in a dosage of 0.2-1.0 weight-% of cement +
SCM, either in the mixing water or after water have been added to the concrete.
==PAGE:57==
CHAPTER 5
Mixing of SCC
This chapter briefly summarizes the procedures regarding mixing SCC in the laboratory
at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). A thorough description can be found in
Appendix D.
5.1
Registration of Moisture Contents in Aggregates
An important part of mixing concrete is to register the current moisture contents in
the aggregates (sand and stone) so the mix can be adjusted in order for the aggregates
to be in the condition called Saturated Surface Dry (SSD) - neither absorbing water
nor contributing water to the concrete mixture. If the aggregates are wetter than SSD,
water will have to be subtracted from the otherwise calculated amount of water to the
mix design. Likewise very dry aggregates results in an increased amount of water to the
mix design.
The moisture content is registered by a simple weigh-dry-weigh method. Representative
samples of the different aggregates are put in weighted petri dishes whose weight has
been registered in advance. The weight of the moist aggregate samples are noted, and
the samples are put into a micro wave oven (along with an additional petri dish with
water). The oven runs for full power for 5 minutes. The aggregates are then stirred in
order to release entrapped moisture, and then put into the oven once more. After an
additional 5 minutes the petri dishes are taken out, air cooled, and weighted once more.
The process of drying in the oven, cooling, and weighing is continued until the weight
of the samples are constant. The samples are thus completely dry, and the difference in
weight corresponds to the amount of moisture in the aggregates. See Section A.1.1 in
the appendix for equations for calculating the moisture contents, and Appendix D for a
step-by-step walk-through of the procedure.
5.2
Mixing Procedure
After adjusting the mix design in accordance with the registered moisture contents of
the aggregates, the mixing can commence. Prior to the mixing all materials are readied
in the proximity of the mixer itself. Figure 5.1 shows the complete setup just prior to
mixing. Aggregates, additives, and all equipment for subsequent testing are set up.
==PAGE:58==
42
5 Mixing of SCC
Figure 5.1:
All aggregates, additives, and equipment are readied next to the mixer prior
to mixing.
From left: Buckets with the weighted sand, stone, cement, fly ash, and water.
Thereafter the mixer. On the slump flow table are canisters with the additives, the slump flow
cone, and a rod. Next to it are readied the small (1 liter) and large (8 liter) pressuremeters,
the Proctor Kit and the insulating slabs for the box for the cubes. On the floor from left is two
buckets with 8 mm mesh sieves for the Proctor test, a pallet with buckets for the Knead Bag
Test and the sieved through specimens for the Proctor tests. Lastly the insulated box with the
four forms for the cubes.
The mixing procedure is as follows:
1. Moist the 200 liter compulsory mixer and swipe away any excess water.
2. Fill larger stone (8-16) subsequently smaller stone (4-8) into the mixer.
3. Pour cement and fly ash into the mixer on top of the stone. Even it out so it covers
the stone.
4. Add half of the sand on top of the cement and fly ash. Make sure that it covers the
cement and fly ash so that only the sand is visible. This is to ensure no cement or
fly ash will spill out over the side (due to its light weight) when mixing commences.
5. Dry-mix for 30 seconds.
6. Stop the mixer and add water. Withhold a bit of the water to rinse the canisters
containing the additives in order to make shure all additives are added. Note the
time. This moment is the basis for all calculations of maturity.
7. Start the mixer.
8. After 30 seconds of mixing the plasticizer (20N) is added trough the lid of the the
mixer while it continues to run. Rinse the container that held the plasticizer with
some of the withheld water. Pour everything into the mixer.
==PAGE:59==
5.3 Testing the Fresh SCC
43
9. After an additional 30 seconds the air entrainment (22SB) is added trough the lid
in the same way.
10. Let the mixer run for 60 seconds before adding the superplasticizer (SKY631) in
order to ensure that superplasticizer and air entrainment are not in direct contact
as this might cause either of the additives to not work as desired.
11. Add any surplus amount of the withheld water into the mixer.
12. After 30 seconds of mixing after adding the suplerplasticizer the mixer is stopped.
13. Add the rest of the sand and let the mixer run for an additional 60 seconds.
14. Stop the mixer. The total mixing time after adding water is 3 minutes and 30
seconds. The SCC is now mixed and ready for testing.
This procedure was followed for all six experiments and are based on the 10 preliminary
experiments conducted prior to the final experiments. The preliminary experiments are
described in Chapter 6 and the lessons learned based on them are summarized. Each
preliminary experiment is thoroughly described in Appendix E.
5.3
Testing the Fresh SCC
The fresh SCC is tested for slump flow, air contents, and density before the experiments
are initiated. This is to ensure the batch fulfill the requirements of the striven SCC
properties so that it can be compared to the other results. Furthermore the temperature
of the freshly mixed SCC is registered.
5.3.1
Slump Flow Test
The first thing to do when the SCC is mixed is to measure the slump flow. The slump
flow indicates the measure of yield stress of the SCC whereas a ‚ÄĚt50‚ÄĚ or V-funnel test
indicates the viscosity. Based on Aalborg Portsland‚Äôs recommendation only the slump
flow test is used since the other two tests are more complicated to perform and are
rarely used. Furthermore the recipe only sets demands for a specific slump flow and not
a specific viscosity.
It is done on the specially designed slump flow table cf. DS/EN 12350-8. The table is
moisten and excess water wiped of. A slump flow cone (witch has also been moisten)
is placed centralized on the metal plate of the table. The cone is filled with fresh SCC,
the top leveled off with the rod, and the table wiped clean for excess SCC. The cone is
vertically lifted so the SCC flows out on the table. When the SCC does not flow any
longer the diameter of the SCC is measured three places. The mean is the slump flow.
An exact walk-through of the test is described in Section D.4 in the appendix. Figure
5.2 shows the slump flow test.
==PAGE:60==

with freshly mixed SCC. Surplus SCC is removed
from cone and table.
(b) The cone is lifted and the diameter of the
SCC is measured. This is the slump flow.
Figure 5.2: (a) left, (b) right.
5.3.2
Air Contents and Density of Fresh SCC
The air contents of the fresh SCC and fresh mortar (SCC sieved through a 4 mm mesh)
are tested with an 8 liter and 1 liter pressuremeter (Air Contents Meter) respectively.
Before initiating the test the buckets of the pressuremeters are weighted. Then the SC-
C/mortar is filled into the buckets and filled to the rim. With a rubber hammer the
bucket is hit five times on four sides in order to release entrapped air. Excess SCC/-
mortar is removed by leveling the bucket off with a rod. The rim is whipped with a wet
paper or a wet sponge. The exactly full bucket is weighted once more and subtracting
the weight of the empty bucket results in the exact weight of the SCC/mortar which
can then be used to find the density by using the volume of the bucket.
The top of the pressuremeter can now be put onto the bucket. The valves are used
to fill boiled demineralized water into the pressuremeter, and after completely filling
it, the valves are closed, the handle on top is pumped, and the air contents measured
by pressing the botton on top. A thorough description of the process can be found in
Section D.5 in the appendix. Figure 5.3 shows the pressuremeter test in process.
==PAGE:61==
5.3 Testing the Fresh SCC
45
(a) Demineralized water is poured into the pres-
suremeter.
(b) The hand of the display is adjusted to be over
exactly the red line before the test is initiated.
Figure 5.3: (a) left, (b) right.
