CHAPTER 6
Experiments
Working with this Master‚Äôs thesis a number of different methods have been used to
determine the setting time of the two SCC recipes, and the correlation between them
are clarified. The full-automatic Vicat Needle Test has been used, the manual Proctor
Penetration Test, and the rather subjective Knead Bag Method have all been used to de-
termine the initial and final setting time of the same SCC batches. Cubes have been cast
in order to visualize the correlation between temperature development and compressive
strength. Based on the strength of the cubes the setting times have been calculated as
well. Lastly full-scale experiments have been conducted where two 6.0 meter high walls
were cast and the formwork pressure measured to show the correlation between setting
time and formwork pressure.
The laboratory experiments were based on the lessons learned of ten preliminary exper-
iments in which the execution of the final six experiments was derived. The following
sections describe the setup of the experiments and the last section is a summary of the
lessons learned during the preliminary experiments.
The full-scale experiment with formwork pressure is addressed lastly in this chapter.
6.1
Laboratory Experiments
A main hypothesis of the proposal to this Master‚Äôs thesis was to show that setting time
is highly influential on the formwork pressure when casting walls with SCC. The first
task of the thesis was to investigate the different methods of determining setting time.
The three most used methods was decided to be investigated, and the setting time from
each method was determined from the same batches of SCC in order to clarify the exact
correlation between the different methods.
The first method is the fully automatic Vicat Needle Test. This test‚Äôs advantage is the
fully automated prospect which ensures directly comparative results without having to
take human interference into account. That being said the test is not necessarily the
most correct due to the relative big impact sand grains can have on the needle penetra-
tion and thus the results. This will be discussed in Chapter 8.
The second method is the Proctor Penetration Test. This test is ‚ÄĚfield friendly‚ÄĚ i.e. easy
==PAGE:64==
48
6 Experiments
to conduct in the field though time-consuming it may be. This method is manual but
rather straight forward and not many factors influence the results.
The third method is the German developed Knead Bag Method. This method is by far
the easiest to conduct, but also the one most influenced by the person conducting the
test. It depends on the person being familiar with the test and to have a good perception
of how to press with exactly 5 kg with a finger. Even though it seems the least scientific
method the German standard DIN 18218 is based on it, and thus all formwork pressure
calculations in Germany. Furthermore several other countries rely on this method in
lack of own national standards regarding this topic. It must thus be assumed that the
method is conservative (safe) if not accurate.
Appendix F contains a walk-through of the three methods and the way to conduct
them is thoroughly described. This chapter summarizes the advantages, disadvantages,
method, and setup, and the results are presented in Chapter 7.
In addition to the above mentioned methods of setting time determination each batch
of SCC contributed to the casting of 12 cubes of 150 mm x 150 mm x 150 mm. These
cubes were tested for their compressive strength at an early stage in order to determine
the strength development and the dependency on the SCC‚Äôs temperature. To ensure an
exact measurement of the developed heat, the cubes were placed in an insulated box.
These experiments were also meant to try Kristensens thesis mentioned in Chapter 2
and in [Kristensen (2011)].
All strengths and setting times have been adjusted with regards to the temperature of
the specimens, thus using the SCCs maturity as a time factor instead of the actual time
since casting (since adding water during mixing).
Photos of all specimens can be seen in Section G.7 in the appendix and the results are
shown in Chapter 7.
6.1.1
Vicat Needle Test
The automatic Vicat Needle Test is a widely used method for determining setting time for
mortars and pastes. It is the basis of the Danish standard DS/EN 196-3:2016 regarding
this topic and thus the most used method for laboratory experiments regarding setting
time in Denmark. The method is not applicable for concrete since stone aggregates
will affect the results. This is taken into account by sieving stone from the concrete so
only the mortar remains. Sand grains (0-4 mm) might still influence the results but the
method is accepted by Aalborg Portland as a method of measuring the setting time of
concretes and thus used in this Master‚Äôs thesis. The apparatus used is called Vicatronic
and produced by the Italian company Matest.
==PAGE:65==
6.1 Laboratory Experiments
49
6.1.1.1
Method
The Vicat Needle Test works by placing a mould with a mortar on a glass plate and
submerging it into a container filled with temperate (approximately 20¬įC) water. This
setup is then put it into the apparatus. A vertically movable needle of a specific di-
mension and weight (see Appendix F) is placed above the mould, and with pre-decided
intervals the needle is released to fall down and penetrate the hardening mortar. After
86 penetrations the test is over and the depth of every penetration is printed. Depend-
ing on the standard the setting time is determined as the time at which the needle only
penetrates to a specific depth. Each penetration is done with a precise distance to the
previous, and each penetration is thus in an untouched part of the specimen. By setting
up an identical setup next to the Vicat apparatus and placing a thermo element into
the middle of this specimen, the temperature development of the hardening mortar is
logged, and the results can be modified from actual time to maturity. This makes the
result comparative to other batches where the ambient temperature might diverge.
6.1.1.2
Setup
Figure 6.1 shows the setup of the Vicat Needle Test experiments.
Figure 6.1: Setup of the Vicat Needle Test experiment. The Vicatronic on the left has the
actual specimen to be tested, while the setup on the right has cast in a thermo element for logging
the temperature development of the specimen, the ambient water, and the air temperature.
After mixing a SCC a suitable amount is sieved through a 4 mm mesh in order to extract
only mortar and leave out 4-8 mm stone and 8-16 mm stone. It is important that the
mortar is from sieved SCC and not a prepared mortar cf. [ASTM (2016)] p. 2. To make
sure the water does not affect the experiment, temperate demineralized water is used
for the bucket in which the specimen is submerged into. For all experiments 9 minute
==PAGE:66==
50
6 Experiments
intervals are used between each needle penetration, and the Vicatronic is started 2-3
hours after water was added during the mixing of the SCC.
6.1.2
Proctor Penetration Test
The Proctor Penetration Test defines the setting time of a concrete by its penetration
resistance cf. DS 423.17. The penetration resistance is measured with a Proctor Pen-
etrometer apparatus that consists of a T-shaped handle that presses a plunger into the
hardening concrete and the applied force is registered. When the penetration resistance
exceeds 3.5 MPa the concrete has reached initial setting. The Proctor Penetration Test
is an easily applicable test to conduct in the field, i.e. on the construction site. In spite
of this it seems as though it is not widely used within the civil engineering contractors
in Denmark.
6.1.2.1
Method
The Proctor Penetrometer has a number of different sized plunger heads. Starting with
the largest head, the plunger is pressed into the fresh concrete. The plunger is pressed
25 mm into the concrete in 10 seconds, and the applied force is read of the handle.
Whenever the applied force exceeds 20 kg the plunger head is changed to a smaller
size. The applied force is divided by the area of the plunger in order to calculate the
penetration resistance (measured in [MPa]). The test is conducted every 15 minutes
until a strength of 5 MPa penetration resistance is reached. The initial setting of the
concrete is then defined as the time at which the penetration resistance is equal to or
exceeds 3.5 MPa. Figure 6.2 shows the Proctor Test in progress.
Figure 6.2:
The Proctor Penetrometer is pressed into the sieved mortar and the applied
pressure divided by the plunger area equals the penetration strength.
==PAGE:67==
6.1 Laboratory Experiments
51
6.1.2.2
Setup
The Proctor kit used for this Master‚Äôs thesis is Matest‚Äôs S088 Proctor Penetrometer with
a spring load of 0-100 kg. Five plunger heads are included in the kit and have an area
of 32, 65, 160, 325, and 650 mm2 respectively. The smallest plunger is not used since
preliminary experiments, along with the recommendation from the company which lent
out the kit, showed some amount of uncertainty when using this plunger. Figure 6.3
shows the Proctor Penetrometer. A thorough walk-through of the experiment can be
found in Appendix F.
Figure 6.3: Matest‚Äôs Proctor Penetrometer kit used for the Proctor Penetration Test.
6.1.3
Knead Bag Method
The Knead Bag Method is a German developed method to estimate the setting time of
concrete based on DIN 18218:2010. The advantage of using this approach is that the
simple setup involved in the method can be applied anywhere, the disadvantage is that
the method is rather subjective as it hinges on the estimation of the individual who
performs the test. The test simply consists of pressing a finger into a bag within which
the fresh concrete is poured. Depending on the consistence and depth of the pressing
the setting is estimated.
6.1.3.1
Method
Fresh concrete is poured into a plastic bag which is then concealed. Every half hour a
finger is pressed into the side of the bag. The finger should be pressed with a force of
5 kg. The test operator then estimates the consistence (when the concrete is still fresh)
or measures the depth of which he pressed the finger (when the concrete is hardening)
according to Table 6.1.
==PAGE:68==
52
6 Experiments
Table 6.1: Description of the setting condition of SCC using the knead bag method.
Consistency:
Description:
Fluid
Flows in the bag
Soft
Does not flow, oozes when pressed
Plastic
Kneadable
Stiff
Can be indented to a max. depth of 30 mm
Semi-firm
Can be indented to a max. depth of 10 mm
Set
Can be indented to a depth of less than 1.0 mm
When the concrete has reached the ‚ÄĚset‚ÄĚ stage the time since adding water, tE,knead, is
noted. The final setting is then defined as tE = tE,knead ¬∑ 1.25.
6.1.3.2
Setup
When the fresh concrete is mixed 8 liters are poured into a 20 liter plastic bag and put
into a 10 liter bucket. The bag is closed and the temperature of the concrete is either
measured with laser on the outside of the bag or by casting in a thermo element (which
was done in this Master‚Äôs thesis). The bucket is then placed on an insulated slab and
the test can begin. Figure 6.4 shows the setup of the Knead Bag Method. Appendix F
thoroughly walks through the setup and execution of the method.
Figure 6.4: Setup of the knead bag method.
==PAGE:69==
6.1 Laboratory Experiments
53
6.1.4
Compressive Strength Experiments
In order to clarify the correlation between the compressive strength of the cast SCC
cubes and the corresponding temperature of the SCC (and thus the maturity), 12 cubes
were cast from each batch of SCC from the six experiments (three with A35 and three
with M30).
Since the focus of the experiment was to determine the early strength
development of the cubes they were removed from their forms as early as possible. A
series of preliminary experiments lead to the discovery that when the Proctor Penetration
Test showed a penetration resistance of approximately 1 MPa the first cubes could be
tested. If the cubes were demoulded earlier the cubes would fall apart because of lack
of strength. The first eight cubes were then tested for compressive strength. One were
tested every half hour (cubes #1-#8). After that the logged temperatures were used
to calculate the maturity of the remaining cubes, and when a maturity of 1 day was
achieved cubes #9 and #10 were tested. The last two cubes (#11 and #12) were tested
after 28 days of maturity. In order to control and time the maturity development of the
last two cubes they were placed in water basins of 20¬įC and 40¬įC. Each of the cubes‚Äô
storage history can be found in Appendix G, and the formulas used for the calculation
of the maturities can be found in Chapter 3.
6.1.4.1
Casting
The 150 x 150 x 150 mm cubes were cast in four specially made wooden forms contain-
ing three cubes in each. After mixing the SCC it was placed into the forms with shovel
by letting it drop from approximately 30 cm height. After filling the forms almost to
the top a rubber hammer was used to hit the form five times on each side of all three
cubes. This process was applied in order to release air and avoid air bubbles in the
cubes. When laying out SCC during big castings the SCC will either be dropped from
a truck, pumped, or otherwise worked so that entrapped air is released without actual
vibration. The application of rubber hammers proved to be an efficient procedure, as
preliminary experiments, without the use of rubber hammers, lead to the creation of air
bubbles that weakened the structural integrity of the cubes.
After the form was hit with rubber hammers, the forms were filled to the top so that
the cubes had the correct height. In three of the 12 cubes thermo elements were cast
in. All cubes were lightly ‚ÄĚjuttet‚ÄĚ on top in order to obtain an even surface. When the
forms were filled they were put in an insulated box (with 80 mm expanded polystyrene
on the bottom, sides, and top) to control the hardening process and calculate the exact
maturity of the cubes. Details regarding the casting of the cubes can be found in Section
F.2.1 in the appendix. Figure 6.5 shows the newly filled forms with SCC.
==PAGE:70==
54
6 Experiments
Figure 6.5: The 12 newly cast cubes in the insulated box. Three cubes have cast in thermo
elements for the registration of temperature development.
6.1.4.2
Setup
When the cubes were ready for testing they were taken out of their forms and pho-
tographed for documentation. The dimensions of the cubes were checked so that the
exact area of the cubes were used to calculate the compressive strength. The cubes
were then placed centralized in the testing machine. For cubes #1-#8, which had a
low strength, an Instron 5500R (100 kN max load) testing machine were used. For the
higher strength cubes (#9-#12) a TONI 3000 (3000 kN max load) machine were used.
Figure 6.6 shows the setup of the Instron machine, Figure 6.7 the TONI machine, and
Figure 6.8 (A) and (B) a cube before and after testing.
Figure 6.6: Setup of the Instron 5500R machine.

before testing commences.

6.1.5
Preliminary Experiments
The purpose of the preliminary experiments (P.E.) was to become familiar with the
testing equipment Vicatronic, to try the testing methods using the Proctor equipment,
and the compressive strength testing machines TONI and Instron. Furthermore the
purpose was to get routine in mixing SCC and casting the test specimens as correctly as
possible. Prior to the preliminary experiments Unicons‚Äô mixing facility in Aved√łre was
visited in order to see how they performed their tests (see Appendix O). This section
shortly summarizes the lessons learned during the total of ten preliminary experiments.
A thorough walk-through of the preliminary experiments and their specific results can
be found i Appendix E and Chapter 7. The basis of the preliminary experiments were
discussed in one of the first supervisor meetings and can be seen in Appendix H. All
agendas and resumes of the supervisor meetings can be found in Appendix P.
The first lesson regarded the setup of the Vicatronic automatic Vicat Needle Test ap-
paratus. A bucket had to be cut in order to allow the standardized cylindrical shaped
Vicat mould to be submerged into water. A sample which was not submerged into water
would dry out during the nearly 13 hours of testing. This resulted in completely different
setting times because full hydration could not take place. Different intervals of testing
was tried as well: 5, 9, 10 and 12 minute intervals. The 5 minute intervals gave a more
precise plot during the few crucial hours where the mortar were setting, but resulted in
the test ending before the mortar was completely set. 12 minutes gave too few points.
The final penetration interval was set to 9 minutes.
Figure 6.9 shows the difference between two mixes tested in a Vicat setup both with a
sample submerged into water and to a sample unsubmerged.

As Figure 6.9 shows, PE7 had a w/cm-ratio = 0.43 while a wrongly mixed SCC resulted
in a w/cm-ratio = 0.48 for PE6. A non submerged sample will risk drying out during
==PAGE:73==
6.1 Laboratory Experiments
57
the testing time and thus achieve a w/cm-ratio ‚Č•0.42. If this happens full hydration
might not take place, [Kielsgaard Hansen (2008)] p. 60. A mix with w/cm-ratio of 0.43
is therefore much more vulnerable than a mix with a w/cm-ratio of 0.48. This can be
seen in Figure 6.9 where the final setting for PE7 is achieved 2 hours and 51 minutes
later if not submerged into water, whereas no difference seems to appear for the two
samples (submerged and not submerged) in mix PE6 due to the high w/cm-ratio.
Other early experiences were that the amount of additives was difficult to add precisely
due to the small amounts of mortar/SCC mixed. Additionally when mixing large quan-
tities of SCC it was difficult to get everything out of the canister containing the exact
amount of additive. The solution was to withhold a small amount of the water that has
to be added to the mix and then use it to rinse the canisters in order to add all of the
additives.
When measuring the amount of moisture in the sand the preliminary experiments showed
that taking only one petri dish of sand was not very representative. By the end of the
preliminary experiments either two or three petri dishes were taken when measuring the
moisture contents of the sand. In every case the sand was mixed around in order to get
a representative sample, and if the sand was kept in different barrels/boxes at least one
representative sample was taken from every barrel/box.
The initial intention was that every mix for execution of all experiment should have a
size of 160 liters of freshly mixed SCC. Even though the mixer at DTU was supposed to
hold 200 liters of concrete, the preliminary experiment showed that 160 liters of unmixed
SCC (aggregates, cement and fly ash) was too big an amount for the mixer to dry-mix.
The amount was thus scaled down to 120 liters by deciding to only cast two buckets for
the Proctor Penetration Test instead of three. With an amount of 120 liters SCC, the
mixer still had trouble with dry-mixing stone, sand, cement, and fly ash which should be
mixed for 30 seconds before adding water and subsequently additives. The preliminary
experiments showed that when adding the additives the mix would be much more fluent
which lead to the decision that the mixing procedure was changed to the following:
1. Add larger stone (8-16 mm) to the mixer,
2. Add smaller stone (4-8 mm) thereafter,
3. Add cement and fly ash,
4. Add half of the sand,
5. Dry-mix for 30 seconds,
6. Stop mixer and add water,
7. Mix for 30 seconds,
8. While continue to mix add plasticizer (20N),
==PAGE:74==
58
6 Experiments
9. After 30 seconds add air entrainment (22SB),
10. After 60 seconds add superplasticizer (SKY631),
11. After 30 seconds stop the mixer and add the rest of the sand,
12. Mix for additional 60 seconds resulting in a total mixing time of 3 minutes and 30
seconds after adding water.
The decision to add cement and fly ash before sand was based on the experience of the
first mixes in the big mixer where cement and fly ash would be pushed over the top of
the mixer when dry-mixing was initiated. This was due to the light weight of the cement
and fly ash, so in order to make sure everything would stay in the mixer, the heavier
sand was put on top of cement and fly ash.
The prolonged mixing time between air entrainment and superplasticizer was based
on recommendation from the external supervisor and specialist from Aalborg Portland.
Mixing the additives before adding it to the mix might influence the additives in a way
so that they do not affect the SCC mix in the desired way. Furthermore the air entrain-
ment might not work as desired if superplasticizer is added too soon.
Some of the first cubes that were cast were cut in half in order to investigate the homo-
geneity of the specimens. It showed many air bobbles why it was decided to hit the forms
with a hammer five times on both sides in order to release the air. This procedure was
assumed to imitate the force of impact when laying SCC i.e. dropping it from a truck,
hose, or similar. Figure 6.10 shows a cube from one of the preliminary experiments (left),
one from the final experiments (middle) cut in half, and a reference cylinder from Unicon.
Figure 6.10: A cube from a preliminary experiment (left) and from a final experiment (center)
both cut in half. The lack of hammering on the forms during the preliminary experiment is
clear: Air bobbles are visible on the left cube, almost none on the center. A cylinder from
Unicon is shown as comparison to the right. Larger air bubbles indicated by red circles.
The first cubes were tested in the compressive strength machine TONI 3000, which is
a testing apparatus capable of adding a load of 3000 kN to a specimen. Because of the
==PAGE:75==
6.1 Laboratory Experiments
59
early stage of the cubes when testing cubes #1-#8 failure occurred before the TONI
could register the exact amount of load added. It was thus chosen to use an Instron
5500R testing machine with a max load of 100 kN instead. When testing the cubes of
1 maturity day (#9 and #10) and 28 maturity days (#11 and #12) the TONI was used.
Figure 6.11 shows the different measurements when using the TONI machine and the
Instron machine at the early stages of strength testing. Between 600 and 800 minutes
of maturity the measured compressive strength of the same SCC (two different batches)
are between 1 and 6 MPa.

The objective of the cubes experiments is to visualize as long a period of time as possible
in the earliest stage possible of the hardening process. Instead of testing several cubes at
the same time, as would be a traditional way to perform tests, it was chosen to test one
cube every 30 minutes in order to visualize the development of the strength. 15 minute
intervals did not show a big enough development, and 1 hour intervals was too long.
==PAGE:76==
60
6 Experiments
6.2
Formwork Pressure Experiments
In order to investigate formwork pressure using SCC and check if there is a correla-
tion between setting time and formwork pressure, two full-scale experiments have been
conducted. Two walls have been cast with SCC: One wall (A) as fast as practically
possible and another (B) as slow as possible given the circumstances. Pressure sensors
were mounted in the formwork in order to continuously log the formwork pressure and
a number of thermo elements were cast into the walls in order to log the temperature
development of the SCC (and thus calculate the maturity of the walls) and to track the
height of the SCC throughout the casting process.
6.2.1
Considerations Regarding Setup
As elucidated in Chapter 2 many factors affect the formwork pressure when casting walls
with SCC. In this Master‚Äôs thesis the objective was firstly to determine the maximum
formwork pressure at different rates of casting using SSC by maintaining all the rheo-
logical parameters and casting methods, secondly to see how these results comply with
the traditionally used formulas for calculating formwork pressure, and lastly to show if
there is a correlation between the setting time of the most applicable of the two given
SCCs (A35 or M30). The most relevant of the two SCCs is the A35 which the laboratory
experiments showed has the quickest setting time.
Other interesting parameters to investigate is the casting method (pumping from dif-
ferent heights through the side of the formwork for instance) which this Master‚Äôs thesis
unfortunately did not have the time to investigate. A list of propositions to future stud-
ies are listed in Section 9.1.
The formwork pressure experiment conducted in this Master‚Äôs thesis had some restric-
tions with regard to the execution. Most important only five pressure sensors were at
disposal (developed by the Technological Institute of Denmark (TI)). Furthermore only
one truck of maximum 7.5 m3 SCC could be delivered. In order to illuminate any dif-
ference between the rate of casting, at least two test specimens would have to be cast.
Two specimens were chosen based on the available amount of SCC whereas two pressure
sensors would be mounted in specimen A (fast casting rate) and three sensors in speci-
men B (slow casting rate). The slow casting was the most interesting since the influence
of the setting time hopefully would be more clear in this casting, why three pressure
sensors were chosen to be in this specimen.
There were many considerations regarding the geometry of the test specimens prior to
the execution of the formwork pressure experiment. The internal dimension (thickness)
of the specimen was chosen to 300 mm based on a common thickness for in-situ cast
walls. This dimension would also be favourable regarding the minimum influence of the
SCC‚Äôs rheology, cast-in parts (reinforcement bars, sensors, etc.), and the friction resis-
tance from the formwork on the SCC. Due to the economic aspect it was first planned
==PAGE:77==
6.2 Formwork Pressure Experiments
61
to use a pipe with a smooth surface as the ‚ÄĚformwork‚ÄĚ. In that way several man-hours
could be saved during construction of an otherwise complex traditional formwork. How-
ever this idea was rejected since the transferability of the results would be questionable
- circular profiles (pipes) might not be comparable with a rectangular profile (walls).
Using a pipe would also cause problems when it came to fastening against overturning
and buoyancy, attachment of thermo elements, and mounting of pressure sensors. It
was therefore chosen to make a section of a wall with the height of 6000 mm and with
a length of 1500 mm. This height was chosen because it was a realistic height (two
floors) and in order to comply with the results from earlier experiments described in the
literature study (Chapter 2). The length of the walls was chosen based on the amount
of available fresh SCC. Prior to the experiment it was informed by the pump division
in Unicon that between 0.5-1.0 m3 SCC should be calculated to be used just to fill the
pump. Therefore only 6.5 m3 SCC was available. Choosing a length of 1500 mm would
give a volume equal to 2.7 m3 (1500 mm x 300 mm x 6000 mm) for each wall.
No reinforcement was used in the walls since this according to Section 3.4.4 would re-
duce the fresh concrete pressure significantly and hereby give a misleading result. A
reinforcement bar was though placed in the center of each wall (from bottom to top)
in order to attach the thermo elements. Likewise a stirrup embedded in the slap was
lapped into the bottom of the walls to secure the walls against overturning after removal
of the formwork. Even though casting from the bottom of the formwork, through a
pump inlet connection, is the most applied casting method when using SCC in vertical
castings (cf. section 2.1.3.3), studies have shown that this method leads to hydrostatical
or even higher pressure on the formwork (see Literature Study). Because of this, casting
SCC from above the formwork was chosen in this experiment.
The location of the experiment was decided to be in a corner of an active construction
site where materials, workforce, and support were available. It was decided that the
walls should be torn down afterwards and thus not be a part of a permanent structure.
This decision was made because no guarantee could be made for the safety and quality
of both the execution and completed construction.
It was strived to achieve a casting rate of 7 m/h for the fast wall and 2 m/h for the slow.
This would enable a direct comparison to former investigations (see Chapter 2). The
limitations of the experiment regarding the available time of a concrete pump resulted
in an average casting rate of 6.0 m/h and 3.6 m/h for wall A and wall B respectively.
6.2.2
Experimental Setup and Execution
The formwork for the two walls with the dimensions 6000 mm x 1500 mm x 300 mm was
build according to the blue prints shown in Appendix I. A caveat to the above is that
the plywood with a thickness of 18 mm and with 13 layers was used instead of the 21
mm (15 layers) as indicated. Due to the lack of knowledge prior to the experiment the
==PAGE:78==
62
6 Experiments
formwork was designed to resist a hydrostatical pressure for a SCC with a density of 25
kN/m2 multiplied with a partial coefficient of 1.4 (equal to 210 kN/m2). Wall A has the
pressure sensors placed 0.5 meters and 2.5 meters from the bottom of the formwork. For
wall B has the pressure sensors placed 0.5 meters, 2.5 meters, and 4.5 meters from the
bottom of the formwork. Both walls has thermo elements placed for every 500 mm in
the full height of the wall. Figure 6.12 shows a sketch of the two walls and the placement
of the pressure sensors and thermo elements. Furthermore 6.5 mm holes were drilled
every half meter throughout the total height of the walls. This was in order to have a
visual marker for the height of the SCC during casting, and to ensure the correct rate of
casting. When the surface of the SCC would reach a hole a small amount of SCC would
pour through before a stone would block the opening.
Figure 6.12: Sketch showing the placement of pressure sensors and thermo elements in wall
A and wall B.
Before the pressure sensors and the temperature loggers were mounted, they were cal-
ibrated. The temperature loggers according to Appendix J and the pressure sensors
according to TI‚Äôs own procedures (which are restricted and thus not attached to this
Master‚Äôs thesis). The pressure sensors shown in Figure 6.13 (a) were mounted to the
formwork from the outside by drilling a √ł50 mm hole and screwing it in place, as dis-

A 24 meter long pump was used to cast the walls. Upon arrival at the construction site
==PAGE:81==
6.2 Formwork Pressure Experiments
65
the concrete truck rotated the drum for 5 minutes before pumping commenced. This
was in order to ensure an even mix of the SCC. A bucket was filled with SCC for which
to perform slump flow test, pressuremeter test, Proctor experiment, Vicat test, and cast
cubes. Meanwhile the walls were cast according to Figure 6.17 in the following order:
1. Wall B (slow) filled up to 1.5 meters height as fast as possible.
2. Wall A (fast) filled up to 3.0 meters height in 20 minutes.
3. Wall B filled up to 3.0 meters mark as fast as possible.
4. Wall A filled up to 6.0 meters mark in 20 minutes.
5. Wall B filled up to 6.0 meters mark in 25 minutes.
Figure 6.17: Order of casting. In order to cast the two walls with the desired speed and at
the same time the walls were filled in steps. The total average speed resulted in rates of casting
of 6.0 m/h for wall A and 3.6 m/h for wall B.
The position of both the concrete truck and concrete pump in relation to the to walls
can be seen on Figure 6.18 (a). A hose was mounted on the outlet of the pump to ensure
a limited height of dropping the SCC. When the casting took place a person was located
on the platform of a scissor lift, see Figure 6.18 (b). This person was able to see down
inside the formwork and could in that way command the pump operator with regards to
starting and stopping the casting and to ensure a correct placing of the SCC. A correct
placing meaning that the end of the hose was either submerged no more than 10 cm into
the surface of the SCC, or no more than 30 cm above the SCC surface.

Wall B: 6 meters/((167 ‚ąí68 min)/60 min) = 3.6 m/h
The delivery note and the concrete quality assurance certificate delivered by Unicon can

While the casting went on a Vicat Needle Test and two Proctor Penetration Tests were
performed, and a slump flow test and pressuremeter test were likewise conducted (all in
the same way as in the laboratory, see Appendix D and F). Six cubes were cast in order
to test the compressive strength at 1 day of maturity, 7 days of maturity, and 28 days
of maturity (two cubes at each time). The reason that 7 days of maturity is wished to
be tested is that Unicon makes their own samples that are tested after 7 and 28 days.
After a few days the Testo loggers and pressure sensors where dismounted and the data
extracted. The relatively long waiting was to ensure final setting was achieved and the
temperature increase would have peaked. Figure 6.20 shows the walls after the formwork
was removed.
Figure 6.20: The two walls after removal of the formwork.

CHAPTER 7
Results
This chapter contains the most important results from the laboratory and full-scale ex-
periments. All raw-data used for the data processing can be found on the attached USB
flash drive and all adjustments and fitting of curves can be found in Appendix G. All
results related to the laboratory experiments are denoted with a preceding ‚ÄĚE‚ÄĚ. The
following number indicates the experiment number where no. 1-3 is the mix design from
the recipe A35 and no. 4-6 the mix design from the recipe M30. For the formwork pres-
sure experiment (sometimes refereed to as full-scale experiment) the notation is ‚ÄĚFP‚ÄĚ.
For all setting time experiments the results are represented in the same color scale (E1:
blue, E2: orange, E3: yellow, E4: magenta, E5: green, E6: cyan, and FP: dark red), see
Figure 7.1.
Figure 7.1: Used colormap for the presentation of results: E1: blue, E2: orange, E3: yellow,
E4: magenta, E5: green, E6: cyan, and FP: dark red.
Prior to testing in every laboratory experiment as well as the full-scale formwork pres-
sure experiment the properties of the fresh SCC has been determined. The properties
encompass slump flow, air content, temperature, and density. All measurements can be
found in Section G.4 Figures G.1-G.4.
7.1
Fitting and Adjustments of Results
The results have been fitted and adjusted in order to be able to compare them among
themselves and to compare them to norms and standards. This section shortly walks
through the editing of the results before presenting the final results in the following
section.
7.1.1
Vicat Needle Test Adjustments
When the mortar in the Vicat Needle Test sets the specimen hardens and the needle
should thus not be able to penetrate deeper than the previous penetration. However the
needle might hit grains that causes the needle to penetrate less than otherwise. This
==PAGE:86==
70
7 Results
results in a plot of the penetration depths that vary up and down. Assuming that only
the deepest penetrations are the correct ones - and that the others are when the needle
hits a sand grain - the plot is fitted so that a penetration depth is only accepted if all
following depths are less deep. This also results in a slightly later setting time. The
decision to adjust the results like this was taken with the accept of the Master‚Äôs thesis
supervisors. Figure 7.2 shows the original plot of the penetration depths (dotted lines)
and the fitted plot (fully drawn line) where only the deepest penetrations are accepted.

Figure 7.2: Original output of the Vicat Needle Test results (dotted lines) and the fitted plot
(fully drawn lines) where only the true penetrations are accepted.
E1 is experiment no.
1
(recipe A35), and so forth. FP is the results from the formwork pressure experiment.
The decision to adjust the plots as shown above does not affect the determination of
initial setting as much as it might the final setting. Due to the definition of final setting
occurrence to be when the needle penetrates 0.5 mm or less into the specimen, the
possible variation of the curves in this area may have a big influence on the final setting.
More on this in the discussion (Chapter 8).
7.1.2
Adjusting for Maturity
The setting time is dependent on the temperature development cf. Chapter 3: Theory.
In order to be able to compare different experiments with each other it is thus necessary
to adjust/convert the time into maturity for all results. This is done in accordance with
the previously described theory. Figure 7.3 shows an example of this adjustment. The
figure shows the development of compressive strengths from the cast cubes as a function
of time (dotted lines) and adjusted with regards to maturity (fully drawn lines). As an
example it is seen that the results for experiment no. 6 (E6) is shifted to the right and
located almost exactly on top of experiment no. 4 (E4). This is partly due to a higher
initial temperature of the fresh SCC at the time of mixing (see Figure G.4).

7.1.3
Comparative Cylinder Compressive Strength
The cubes are tested and their compressive strength determined.
However the com-
pressive strengths has to be converted into comparative cylinder compressive strengths.
This is in order to compare with norms and standards which mostly uses compressive
strength of cylinders. The conversion factors are 0.78 for the A35 SCCs and 0.81 for
the M30 SCCs (see Chapter 3) and are based on Table 12 in DS/EN 206:2013. Figure
7.5 shows the cube compressive strengths (dotted lines) and the comparative cylindrical
compressive strength (fully drawn line).

setting based on the measured heat development of a concrete was presented in [Kris-
tensen (2011)]. Based on the temperature measurements of the cubes and the setting
times from the Proctor Penetration Tests a similar comparison can be done. Figure 7.13
shows the heat development from the cubes calculated according to Section 3.1.1. The
specific heat capacity used for the calculation are cc,A35 = 1.02 kJ/kg/¬įC and cc,M30 =
1.03 kJ/kg/¬įC. For simplicity it is assumed that no heat loss took place from the insu-
lated box during the experiment why ‚ąÜQtrans,n in Equation 3.1 is neglected. The times

CHAPTER 8
Discussion
The results presented in Chapter 7 are discussed and assessed in the following chapter.
First the methods for determining the setting time and the overall results are discussed,
followed up by a comparison of the result of the different testing methods.
Last a
discussion of the results from the formwork pressure experiment compared to earlier
studies and its correlation with the determined setting time and the different methods
of calculating maximum pressures are discussed.
8.1
Setting Time Determination
The results for all testing methods clearly shows a similarity in the initial and final
setting for the two recipes A35 and M30. For both initial and final setting, and for all
of the four testing methods, the setting of the three experiments E4-E6 (M30 recipe)
follow the same tendency. The same applies for E1 and E2 (A35 recipe) which develop
at a similar rate. However the E3 mix that should lie within the same magnitude of
E1 and E2 show a later initial and final setting time in comparison. A quality control
of the procedures and amount of components used for both E1, E2, and E3 showed no
deviation in either quantities or procedure, and the adjustment of the mix design with
regards to moisture contents in the aggregates was done correct. However a fairly big
difference in the moisture content of the sand was found: Around 6% difference between
E3 (1.6%) and E1 and E2 (7.1% and 8.8% respectively), see Table G.1 in the appendix.
Even though an adjustment of the water was made for all mixtures to regulate the dif-
ferent moisture content in the aggregates, it is assessed that such a big difference may
be the cause of deviance of the setting times. Maybe due to the admixtures and their
properties or the time of which the aggregates absorb or emit the added water. Due
to the unknown influence of dry vs. moist aggregates Aalborg Portland always uses
oven-dried aggregates to eliminate this source of error. This however was not possible
due to the facilities and time at hand in this Master‚Äôs thesis. No matter the reason for
the setting times of E3 to deviate from E1 and E2, the later setting times are seen in all
the setting time determination methods, and the difference or correlation between the
methods can thus still be reliably addressed.
The formwork pressure experiment (FP) which also was of the A35 recipe showed a gen-
eral later time of setting than E1 and E2. A number of factors might be the cause of this.
First of all the batch was not mixed in the exact same way as in the laboratory, both due
to the automated way the batching plant works and due to the accuracy of measuring
==PAGE:98==
82
8 Discussion
the different components. Also the time from mixing water to casting on the site is quite
different to the procedure in the laboratory. The transportation was approximately 50
minutes whereas the casting in the laboratory was followed directly after mixing. The
chemical processes of hardening of course still take place but the constant mixing might
influence the rate with which the SCC sets. Further more the casting method of pumping
might influence the setting since the chemical binders (hydration) might be shred apart
due to the force of the pump. This could be an interesting subject to further investigate.
The ASTM C403 standard prescribes that the final setting of a given concrete is equal
to when the concrete specimen can resist a proctor penetration resistance of 27.58 MPa
(4000 psi) or have a compressive strength of 0.69 MPa (100 psi). Even though the test-
ing methods seems similar, the stress-state are different which explains the difference in
the definitions. The proctor resembles the Lok-test where both compressive and shear
stresses are present. Cubes on the other hand are tested in a one-dimensional pressure
until failure. The compressive strength until failure for cubes works through the weakest
part of the cube whereas the proctor only tests the resistance in a random point of the
specimen. The risk of this place being the weakest is thus very small.
A table showing all setting times for all methods can be found in the appendix in Section
G.4.6.
8.1.1
Comparison of Setting Time Determination Methods
Of the four methods used in this Master‚Äôs thesis to determine setting time, only the Vicat
Needle Test and the Proctor Penetration Test determine the initial setting. Figure 8.1
show the initial setting determined by the Vicat and the Proctor methods.

==PAGE:99==
8.1 Setting Time Determination
83
Generally the M30 SCC sets later than the A35 SCC. This is mainly due to the lower
amount of cement (20% less) and the increased amount of plasticizer (139% extra) in
the M30 recipe, see Section 4.1. Less cement content results in a lower final strength
and a reduced strength at a given time in the hardening process compared to a SCC
with a lower w/c-ratio. Plasticizers are slightly retarding and an increased amount of
plasticizer results thus in a later setting time.
Figure 8.1 shows that the Proctor method indicates a later time of initial setting than
the Vicat method. Table 8.1 shows the mean difference in initial setting determined by
the Proctor vs. the Vicat method for the A35 recipe, the M30 recipe, and an overall
mean.
Table 8.1: Mean difference between Proctor and Vicat determination of initial setting measured
in maturity minutes. The number notes the mean time the Proctor method determines initial
setting later than the Vicat method. The formwork pressure experiment (FP) is included in
the value for A35.
A35
M30
Overall
Mean difference in maturity minutes
58.8
99.0
76.0
Standard deviation
3.1
31.8
28.4
The table shows that the Proctor method with great accuracy (standard deviation of 3.1
min) for the A35 recipe experiments defines a time of initial setting 58.8 min later that
the Vicat. Regarding the M30 recipe experiments the deviation from the Vicat method
leads to a less accurate mean difference between the Proctor and Vicat methods. Due to
the same inaccuracy the overall difference between the Proctor and the Vicat method is
equally inaccurate (standard deviation of 28.4 min), but as Figures 8.1 and 7.6 indicates
this is due to an earlier time of initial setting for experiment E4.
It should be considered whether the Vicat Needle Test at all is the proper method for
determining setting time of SCCs. As mentioned in Chapter 6 and Section F.1.1.3 the
test method is originally designed for determination of paste setting times and not mor-
tars (DS/EN 196-3). Besides the different definition of initial setting from the standard
DS/EN 480-2, the whole setup differs why it is not possible to say whether or not this
standard would comply better with the results from the Proctor method. A more thor-
ough investigation of the definitions of setting time from the two standards might be
interesting and are recommended for a further study.
Regarding final setting all four methods used in this Master‚Äôs thesis provide a time of de-
termination. Figure 8.2 shows the determined final setting of all laboratory experiments
(E1-E6) and the full-scale formwork pressure experiment (FP) from the Vicat, Proctor,
and Knead Bag methods, and from the compressive strength of the cubes.

Figure 8.2: Final setting determined by Vicat method, Proctor method, from the compressive
strengths of the cubes, and from the Knead Bag method, for all laboratory experiments (E1-E6)
and the full-scale formwork pressure experiment (FP).
The figure shows that no immediate correlation between the different methods can be
found. The Proctor, and especially the Vicat, show the most imprecise determinations of
final setting. Regarding the Vicat this is due to the difficulty of producing a specimen of
exactly 40 mm and thus the varying precision of the final setting occurrence. According
to the Danish standards the Proctor does not have a build-in definition of final setting
occurrence but is extrapolated based on fitted curves which thus makes the definition
more inaccurate. The cubes show the most precise determination of final setting which
must be because of the minimal human influence on the test results and the general
highly accurate results from the compressive strength tests. The knead bag method is
also somewhat consistent but not necessarily accurate. This is because of the 15 min
test intervals when performing the test. The time of final setting is thus only with 15
min precision at best.
8.1.2
Heat Development Method
As Figure 7.13 in Chapter 7 shows the time of initial and final setting corresponds well
with a heat development of 7.1 and 12.6 kJ/h/kg cement respectively based on the six
laboratory experiments (E1-E6). The correlation can be visualized by plotting the time
==PAGE:101==
8.1 Setting Time Determination
85
of setting based on these heat developments as a function of the times of setting from
the Proctor test. A perfect correlation would thus result in a line with a R2 value of
1. Figure 8.3 shows the correlation marked by ‚ÄĚx‚ÄĚ and with plotted trendlines. The R2
values of 0.996 and 0.999 proves an almost perfect correlation between these two methods
of determination. The time of initial setting of 16 kJ/h/kg cement that Kristensen finds
does not correspond well with this experiment‚Äôs initial setting of 7.1 kJ/h/kg cement.
This is however due to the setup of the experiment and the assumptions made regarding
heat loss of the insulated box that contained the specimens. The important part is
that the experiment show an almost perfect correlation between the heat development
and the Proctor test. This shows that a measurement of developed heat from small
specimens can determine the time of setting with great precision and thus render the
use of other inconvenient tests superfluous.

8.1.3
Early Strength Development from Cubes
The compressive strength of the cubes indicate the real strength of the SCC whereas
the other methods only imply a stage of strength from which times of setting can be
determined. The early strength development of the cubes can be used to determine
the final setting, but more interestingly it indicates when the SCC can withstand its
own weight. The cubes cannot be tested before they are demoulded, and cannot be
demoulded before they are able to carry their own weight. The final setting defined by
the compressive strength of the cubes occur according to Figure 7.11 approximately 150
maturity minutes after the first cube was tested. But when the first cube was tested
==PAGE:102==
86
8 Discussion
the SCC had enough strength to not fall apart when demoulded. If a time of setting
should be used to define a time of the formwork pressure to reach zero, a new definition
is required, which would probably lie at the time a cube is able to be demoulded without
falling apart.
Figure 7.11 also shows that the first cubes that are able to be demoulded without falling
apart has a compressive strength of approximately 0.2 MPa. The following cubes show
a rapid development in strength especially after the point of final setting.
As Table 7.2 shows the compressive strength of the cubes after 1 day of maturity and
28 days of maturity are quite precise with the biggest difference between the test results
amounting to 13% of the mean for the cubes of 1 day of maturity, and 10% for the cubes
of 28 days of maturity. According to DS/EN 206 the difference between the results must
not exceed 15% of the mean. The results are thus acceptable according to the standard.
The strengths after 28 maturity days are for A35 and M30 31% and 36% higher than
the designed strength respectively. This is due to the minimum requirements of cement
content in a mix design and is part of the quality assurance of the concrete factories.
The compressive strength of the cubes from the formwork pressure (FP) experiment was
lower than the laboratory experiments. This might be due to the cubes of the FP ex-
periment not being put into the insulated box after casting. The measured temperature
in the core of the cubes would thus show the same (and the maturity be calculated to
the same) but the edge of the cubes might have a lower temperature and thus lesser
strength.
8.2
Formwork Pressure Experiments
The full-scale formwork pressure experiment can be subdivided into three investigations:
The formwork pressure compared to the determined setting times, the measured form-
work pressure compared to previously made experiments and a comparison with five
different formwork pressure formulas, and the development of a new formwork pressure
formula based on the specific full-scale experiment.
8.2.1
Formwork Pressure Compared to Setting Time
Figure 8.4 shows the measured formwork pressure of wall B (slow casting rate) as a
function of maturity. The times of initial and final setting determined by Vicat, Proctor,
Cubes, and Knead Bag method are plotted as vertical black lines. The different stages
of the Knead Bag are plotted as crosses. The red cross indicate the time which the bag
was taken out of the bucket (able to stand by itself) from experiment no. 3. The Knead
Bag test was started later than normal at the formwork pressure experiment due to the
practicalities of the full-scale experiment. Experiment no. 3 resembles the formwork
pressure experiment in all other aspects though, why an indication of when the bag was

able to withhold its own pressure are plausible to be at the same time for the formwork
pressure experiment.

Figure 8.4: Formwork pressure of wall B (slow casting rate) as a function of maturity. The
times of initial and final setting determined with Vicat, Proctor, Cubes, and Knead Bag method
are plotted as black lines. The different stages from the Knead Bag method are plotted a crosses.
The red cross indicate when the bag was taken out of the bucket for experiment E3 which in all
other comparisons resemble the formwork pressure experiment.
The figure shows that all times of setting, initial and final alike, occurs after the form-
work pressure in the bottom of the wall has declined to zero (between 100 and 400
maturity minutes later). This indicates that even though the definitions of initial and
final setting might be necessary variables for the determination of the maximum form-
work pressure, it might be an inappropriate indicator for the determination of when the
formwork pressure has declined to zero. The time at which the bag of the Knead Bag
Method was taken out of the bucket is a much better indicator of when the formwork
pressure starts to decline. This stage in the formwork pressure development could be
interesting to further study.
Figure 8.4 further shows that the formwork pressure in the bottom (0.5 m above ground)
and at the top (4.5 m above ground) does not reach zero at the same time. The SCC
should though theoretically set at the same time due to all the SCC coming from the
same batch, and the formwork pressure should thus reach zero at the same time. The
reason why this is not the case could be because the continues mixing in the concrete
truck affects the time of setting as earlier mentioned. It indicates that the SCC does not
start to harden before it lies still in the form. The time difference in maturity minutes for
when the formwork pressure reaches zero is approximately 390 minutes for the bottom
sensor and 510 minutes for the top sensor. This 120 maturity minutes in difference
==PAGE:104==
88
8 Discussion
almost corresponds to a real time of 90 min which is the time difference between when
the concrete reached the sensors. If the 120 maturity minutes should correspond to 90
min real time the concrete should have a temperature of 26¬įC. The SCC temperature
was measured to 24.4¬įC at the time of casting. It is plausible that formwork added the
extra two degrees Celsius due to the casting took place a warm summer day. If so the
concrete took exactly the same time to reach a formwork pressure of zero from the time
when casting reached the respective heights of 0.5 m and 4.5 m.
8.2.2
Formwork Pressure Compared to Former Experiments and
Calculated Maximum Pressures
Wall B was cast with the A35 SCC at a rate of 3.6 m/h. The slump flow was 640
mm and the density 2300 kg/m3. The maximum formwork pressure 5.5 meters below
the concrete surface was measured to 80.9 kN/m2. Tabel 7.4 showed an expected hy-
drostatical pressure of 124.2 kN/m2 according to method 5. The measured formwork
pressure amount to 65% of the expected. The design value of the formwork is 1.4 times
the hydrostatical pressure and thus 2.14 times as strong as ultimately required. This
endorse the need for optimized methods of designing and calculating formwork pressure
since great economical gains in terms of less reinforcement of the formwork and less
man-hours would be required.
As mentioned in the literature study (Chapter 2) [Galvind & Frederiksen (2001)], [Thrys√łe,
Rasmussen, Berrig, Hasholt, & Pade (2004)], [Thrane (2007)], and [Brameshuber & Ue-
bachs (2003)] all performed similar formwork pressure experiments. Different parameters
were adjusted such as slump flows varying between 600-750 mm and densities varying
between 2300-2350 kg/m3, and different recipes of SCC were used. Walls of different
heights (3.5, 4.0, 5.0, 5.5, and 6.0 m) were cast from the top with different rates of casting
spanning between 2.5-20.0 m/h. The conclusion of the four articles can be summarized
as follows: Despite different compositions of SCC, varying geometry of the walls, and
varying amounts of reinforcement, casting rates of 10-20 m/h with SCC all leads to a hy-
drostatical pressure whereas casting rates of 2-10 m/h only leads to a maximum pressure
of 40-85% of hydrostatical pressure. The experimental setup of [Galvin & Frederiksen
(2001)] is quite similar to this Master‚Äôs thesis setup. They achieve a maximum pressure
of 70% of hydrostatical pressure measured 4.5 meters under the concrete surface at a
casting rate of 2.7 m/h. The studied former investigations all show that casting rates
less than 5 m/h (which are low in the investigations but still fast compared to the rates
of casting of 0.5-1.0 m/h for traditional concrete) result in a maximum formwork pres-
sure below hydrostatical pressure, but also conclude that former studies are needed in
order to reach decisive general conclusions.
The maximum formwork pressure is calculated by use of the five methods mentioned in
Section 3.4. Method no. 1, 2, and 3 are all elderly methods derived before the use of
SCC became widespread. They are all thus meant for formwork pressure calculations for
==PAGE:105==
8.2 Formwork Pressure Experiments
89
traditional concrete. The practise today is to use method no. 4 (DIN 18218) together
with hydrostatical pressure (method 5). The three first methods are though still interest-
ing to compare with. The main issues regarding method no. 1-3 is that they do not take
all relevant parameters into account. Method no. 1 only regards density, rate of casting,
temperature of the concrete, and the geometry of the formwork. Regarding the walls as
columns (due to the definition of the length of the wall to be larger that 2 m in order to
regard is as a wall) the maximum formwork pressure is 81.8 kN/m2. Compared to the
actual measured pressure of 80.9 kN/m2 this method fits acceptable - further investiga-
tions are though required in order to ensure this is not only a coincidence. Method no.
2 takes temperature, rate of casting, height of casting, and geometry of the formwork
into account. Whether or not the walls are regarded as walls or columns the calculated
maximum formwork pressure are too low (57.1 and 75.6 kN/m2). Method no. 2 can
thus not be used for SCCs. Method no. 3 only takes slump flow, temperature, and rate
of casting into account. The maximum calculated formwork pressure of 74.5 kN/m2 is
also too low and this method can neither be used for SCC.
The maximum formwork pressure calculated by method no. 4 (DIN 18218) is based
on the parameters rate of casting, density, temperature, final setting, and potential use
of reinforcement. The height of the formwork is thus not regarded why the calculated
maximum formwork pressure always should be compared to the hydrostatical pressure
(method no. 5). By using the Knead Bag Method to determine the final setting of
the SCC the maximum formwork pressure is calculated to 214.4 kN/m2. Because the
method takes the setting time into account, the determined maximum pressure can be
calculated to occur 9.5 m under the concrete surface (214.4 kN/m2 / 22.586 kN/m2 =
9.5 m). Since the wall only is 6 m high DIN 18218 is disregarded and the hydrostatical
pressure (method no. 5) used. That being said the maximum measured formwork pres-
sure is significantly lower (hydrostatical pressure = 124.2 kN/m2 and max. measured
pressure = 80.9 kN/m2).
Summarized there is use for further testing of method no. 1 (CIRIA report 108:1985)
if it is to be used with SCC, and a smaller safety factor for method no. 4 (DIN 18218)
could be derived in order to optimize the use of this. Alternately a whole new formula
should be derived that better describes the maximum formwork pressure of SCCs.
8.2.3
Development of New Formwork Pressure Equation
When the formwork pressure in the bottom of wall B is plotted as a function of time, and
the decline in pressure that occur during casting brakes are disregarded, the maximum
pressure end up 0.7 kN/m2 above hydrostatical pressure. This verifies that if a form is
filled as fast as possible (from the top) the maximum formwork pressure can be expected
to be equal to hydrostatical pressure. It also shows that just minor breaks in the casting
result in a decline in pressure that will not be added once the casting continues. Figure
8.5 shows this where the fully drawn line is the measured formwork pressure on wall B‚Äôs

disregarding the declines (black lines). The hydrostatical pressure is plotted as a black
dashed line at 124.2 kN/m2 and the maximum achieved pressure of the dotted line is
124.9 kN/m2.

the trendlines for the decline in pressure.
As mentioned in the literature study (Chapter 2) [Leemann & Hoffmann (2003)] in-
vestigates the formwork pressure for SCC when it is pumped from the bottom of the
formwork. This method leads to a nearly hydrostatical pressure, and even though the
casting method deviates from the one used in this Master‚Äôs thesis, the formwork pres-
sure of Leemann & Hoffmann‚Äôs ‚ÄĚSCC 3‚ÄĚ (which resembles SCC A35 with regards to
w/cm-ratio and composition) decline much like wall B in Figure 8.6.
Based on the above considerations of assuming hydrostatical pressure if pressure declines
are disregarded, and the trendline of pressure decline, an equation (Eq. 8.2) can be
derived that describe the hypothetical maximum pressure working on the formwork,
pmax, when the casting height, rate of casting, and density of the SCC is known.
pmax =

The equation is made under the assumption that the SCC have the same properties as

with a tendency according to the average of the slope from the tree trendlines in Figure
8.5 (the second term of Eq. 8.2). The equation can thus be used to either estimate the
maximum formwork pressure or calculate the rate of casting in order to not exceed a

why the equation is not perfectly accurate is due to the pressure decline formula being
a mean of the three pressure decline periods.
==PAGE:109==
CHAPTER 9
Conclusion
In the laboratory six experiments were conducted: Three with a self-compacting con-
crete (SCC) of strength class C35 and aggressive environmental class, and three with a
strength class of C30 and moderate environmental class, called A35 and M30 respectively.
For each experiment both initial and final setting was determined using the following
four methods: The Vicat Needle Test, the Proctor Penetration Test, the Knead Bag
Method, and based on the heat development from the SCC. Based on cubes cast in
each of the laboratory experiments the early strength development of the two SCCs are
identified as well. Lastly a full-scale formwork pressure experiment was conducted where
two walls were cast with the A35 SCC and the rise and decline in formwork pressure
were measured. With the batch from the full-scale experiment similar setting time tests
were performed. Based on the total of seven experiments the following can be concluded:
The Vicat Needle Test showed an initial setting of 429 and 651 maturity minutes for
the A35 and the M30 SCC respectively. The Proctor Penetration Test shows a later
initial setting than the Vicat Needle Test (488 and 750 maturity minutes for A35 and
M30 respectively). For the A35 recipe initial setting occurs 59 maturity minutes later
(with a standard deviation of 3 minutes) and for the M30 recipe it occurs 99 maturity
minutes later (standard deviation 32 minutes). The overall mean difference between the
methods are 76 maturity minutes with a standard deviation of 28 minutes. An exact
difference can thus not be determined with sufficient accuracy due to the Vicat tests
varying precision. This might be because the Vicat test was done in a modified way
not meant for mortars. The Vicat test is thus not recommended for use of setting time
determination of SCCs.
Regarding final setting there can be found no consistent correlation between the Vicat
Needle Test, the Proctor Penetration Test, the Knead Bag Method, and the determi-
nation of final setting based on compressive strength from cubes. This is due to the
inaccuracy of the Vicat tests definition of final setting, the uncertainty of the extrap-
olated final setting from the Proctor method, and the imprecision of the Knead Bag
Method (based on 15 minutes test intervals). The determination of final setting based
on the compressive strength of cubes is assessed to be most accurate (and is most con-
sistent).
Generally the M30 sets later than the A35. This is due to the enlarged w/cm-ratio and
the enlarged amount of plasticizer which has a retarding effect.
==PAGE:110==
94
9 Conclusion
Aalborg Portland‚Äôs new method of determining time of setting based on the measured
heat development of the SCC was tested in a simplified setup. It resulted in a time of
initial setting when the heat development reached 7.1 kJ/h/kg cement and a final set-
ting at 12.6 kJ/h/kg cement. These results does not correspond with Aalborg Portland‚Äôs
time of final setting at 16 kJ/h/kg cement but this is due to the simplified setup where
the heat loss of the setup among other things is disregarded. The linear correlation
between the setting time determined on the basis of the heat development of the SCC
and the Proctor setting times are though R2=0.996 for initial setting and R2=0.999 for
final setting. This proves the correlation between the two methods, and enables an easier
way to determine initial and final setting compared to the other imprecise methods.
The experiments regarding the early strength development of SCC cubes show that the
cubes are able to be demoulded (and thus carry their own weight) when they have a
compressive strength of approximately 0.2 MPa. The cubes show a rapid development
in compressive strength after final setting occurs (at 0.69 MPa). The results from the
compressive strength tests of the cubes after 28 days of maturity lie within the accepted
deviance stated by DS/EN 206 (the biggest difference between two test results amount
to 10% of the mean whereas the standard states it should be less than 15%).
At the full-scale formwork pressure experiment an un-reinforced wall of 6.0 m height, 1.5
m width, and 0.3 m thickness were cast from the top with a rate of casting of 3.6 m/h.
The measurements showed that the formwork pressure in the bottom of the wall was
reduced to zero before any of the used methods indicated either initial or final setting
(between 100 and 400 minutes before). The best indicator for a beginning decline in
formwork pressure was when the Knead Bag could stand by itself. Further investigations
are needed in order to find a correlation between the decline in formwork pressure and
the setting time definitions.
The maximum formwork pressure was measured to 80.9 kN/m2, only 65% of the hydro-
statical pressure of 124.2 kN/m2 which is the most common basis of formwork pressure
calculations today. The maximum pressure of 65% of hydrostatical pressure corresponds
well with past studies done in Denmark and Germany who all showed pressures below
hydrostatical pressure when using casting rates below 5 m/h. Of the five methods for
calculating the maximum formwork pressure two cannot be used for SCC (ACI 347R-04
and [Anl√¶gsteknikforeningen (2011)]), one has to be further investigated (CIRIA report
108:1985), and two are too conservative (DIN 18218 and hydrostatical pressure). A new
formula should be derived based on hydrostatical pressure and the decline in pressure
based on numerous new experiments. Table 9.1 shows an overview of which of the for-
mulas that apply for the conducted formwork pressure experiment.
==PAGE:111==
9.1 Further studies

Eq. 9.1
During every break in the casting the formwork pressure declined with a similar tendency.
Disregarding this tendency the pressure would achieve hydrostatical pressure. Taken the
decline in pressure into account an equation for the maximal formwork pressure (pmax)
can be derived that takes both the density (ŌĀc), the height of the casting (H), and the
rate of casting (v) into account.:
pmax =
[
ŌĀc ¬∑ H
]
‚ąí
[
0.5076 ¬∑ H
v ¬∑ 60
]
(9.1)
This equation only applies for the tested SCC with a slump flow of 640 mm and a casting
temperature of the SCC of 24.4¬įC and is thus a hypothesis that needs further investi-
gations. Further studies of this decline might lead to a general formula for calculating
a more precise maximum formwork pressure when casting walls with SCC, and might
shred some light on the influence of the setting times on the formwork pressure.
9.1
Further studies
A number of interesting questions has arisen during this Master‚Äôs thesis that would be
interesting to investigate in further studies.
Even though the amount of water for a mix design always is adjusted in accordance to
the moisture contents of the aggregates, it would be interesting to investigate whether
or not the initial stage of moisture in the aggregates affect the setting time.
The most accurate definition of when initial setting occurs when using the Vicat Needle
Test could be investigated i.e. the difference between the standards DS/EN 196-3 and
DS/EN 480-2. These investigations could be made for different recipes and for mortars
sieved from mixed concretes/SCCs and for directly mixed mortars to clarify the differ-
ence.
A more general investigation on the decline of formwork pressure would be interesting.
For example by casting a number of pipes of a certain height using different casting
==PAGE:112==
96
9 Conclusion
methods and by adjusting the rheological parameters of the SCC. With a pressure sen-
sor in the bottom of the pipes the decline in pressure could be related to the time of
setting.
Based on the Equation 9.1 different castings could be made in order to prove the relia-
bility of the equation by adjusting casting height, rate of casting, and height of the wall.
Based on these results a more general equation for the decline in formwork pressure
might be derived.
An investigation regarding the influence of transportation time or casting method is
interesting. The time from when water is added to the mix to when it is cast, might
influence the time of setting. This could be due to a continues mixing in a concrete truck
or due to pumping the SCC and thus destroying the internal binders that occur during
the hydration. If so, an equation to calculate the formwork pressure cannot only take
time of setting into account but should also include time of transportation and method
of casting.

APPENDIX A
Additional Theory
This appendix contains additional theory not deemed necessary to include in the main
report but still essential as quality assurance for this Master‚Äôs thesis academic credibility.
A.1
Determination of Moisture Contents in Aggregates
and Adjustment of Water in SCC Mix Design
An important factor when mixing SCC (or any concrete) is to adjust the applied water
in the mix. Aggregates (sand and stone) will have a varying amount of moisture content
which affects the total amount of added water in the concrete. Before determining the
amount of aggregates in a specific mix, it is thus important to determine the moisture
contents of the aggregates, and then adjust the amount of aggregates and water to the
mix design.
A.1.1
Determination of Moisture Contents in Aggregates
The moisture contents in aggregates are found by a simple weigh-dry-weigh method
described in Appendix D Section D.2. The moisture contents is determined by taking
a representative sample and weighing it (a representative sample means a sample not
from the top of the pile of the aggregates - this will most likely be either more or less
moist than the rest of the pile). After weighing the sample it is dried in a microwave
oven to eliminate all water. The completely dry sample is then again weighted, and the
moisture content calculated as such cf. [Gottfredsen & Nielsen (2010)] p. 74:
u = mmoist ‚ąímdry
mdry ‚ąímdish
¬∑ 100%
(A.1)
where u is the moisture content, mmoist is the mass of the moist sample, mdry is the mass
of the dried sample, and mdish is the mass of the petri dish in which the sample is put.
A.1.2
Adjustment of Water in SCC Recipe
A number of factors affects the amount of water to add in a SCC mix. In addition to
the above mentioned moisture contents of the sand and stone aggregates, the water in
the additives has to be taken into account as well. This however does not vary from
==PAGE:118==
102
A Additional Theory
time to time as the moisture contents of the aggregates does.
The recipe before adjustments list the density, amount, and volume of the SCC. With
the desired amount of SCC as a factor the initial amount of each aggregate/additive can
be listed. As an example see Table A.1 below for the amount of cement required for a
final amount of Vdesign = 120 liters A35 SCC:

tables showing the exact amount of moisture in all experiments, see Table G.1.
A.2
Setting Time
As mentioned in the previous section the mixing of water with Portland-cement initiates
a number of reactions between the clinker materials C3S, C2S, C3A, and C4AF and the
==PAGE:120==
104
A Additional Theory
water, i.e. it hydrates. The exact reactions are quite complicated and not fully clarified
as of today, but can be simplified as such according to [Kielsgaard Hansen (2008)] p. 58:
2C3S + 6H
‚ÜíC3S2H3 + 3CH
(A.4)
2C2S + 4H
‚ÜíC3S2H3 + CH
(A.5)
C3A + 6H
‚ÜíC3AH6
(A.6)
C4AF + 2CH + 10H ‚ÜíC3AH6 + c3FH6
(A.7)
These reaction does not take place at the same time or at the same rate however. Figure
A.1 shows the simplified process of hydration.
Figure A.1: Schematic illustration of the hydration process of a cement paste.
The final
setting is defined as the time when the unreacted cement particles and the products hydration
just forms a solid-state skeleton, cf. [Kielsgaard Hansen (2008)].
The aluminates (C3A and C4AF) are known to hydrate at a much higher rate than the
silicates (C3S and C2S). This means that the stiffening and setting characteristics are
highly affected by the hydration reactions consisting the aluminates. The silicas on the
other hand determines the hardening characteristics of the Portland-cement, cf. [Mehta
& Monteiro (2014)] p. 201.
==PAGE:121==
A.3 Calculation of Compressive Strength
105
In general it is known that the setting time for a concrete mainly depends on the 6
factors, [Beton-Teknik (1999)], p. 15:
‚ÄĘ the chemical composition of the cement,
‚ÄĘ the degree of fineness of the cement (Blaine-value),
‚ÄĘ storage conditions of the cement,
‚ÄĘ temperature of the concrete and the surroundings,
‚ÄĘ w/c-ratio,
‚ÄĘ additives.
A.3
Calculation of Compressive Strength
The compressive strength of concrete is defined as the maximum numerical value of the
nominal normal stress in a specimen which is affected by a one-dimensional pressure
until failure, cf. [Beton-Bogen (1985)] p. 133, illustrated in Figure A.2.
Figure A.2: Stress ŌÉ in a specimen affected by an evenly distributed compressive force F over
the cross-section Ac.
The compressive strength of concrete is defined as such cf. DS/EN 12390-3 p. 7:
fc = F
Ac
(A.8)
where fc is the compressive strength in [MPa] (or [N/mm2]) also known as the normal
stress ŌÉ, F is the maximum load when failure occurs measured in [N], Ac is the area of
==PAGE:122==
106
A Additional Theory
the cross-section on which the force impacts measured in [mm2].
The speed with which the specimen is loaded, the shape of the specimen, the temperature,
and humidity are all factors that affects the compressive strength.
As a default all
experiments are conducted in accordance with the international standards DS/EN 12390-
1 and DS/EN 12390-3. These prescribe a speed load of 0.6 ¬Ī 2 MPa/s. With an area of
150 mm x 150 mm this equals a speed measured in kN/s of
0.6MPa
s
= 0.6 N
mm2/s
(A.9)
0.6 N
mm2/s ¬∑ 150mm ¬∑ 150mm = 13500 N
mm2/s = 13.5 N
mm2/s
(A.10)
As a default the load is thus added with a speed of 13.5 kN/s.
This concerns test
specimens of 28 days of maturity though, and not early stage testing as done in this
Masters thesis. As a rule of thumb the failure is wished to occur within 30 sec. Based
on preliminary experiments a load speed of 1.5 kN/s was chosen (see chapter 6 and
appendix F).
