APPENDIX C
SCC Components
The components that make up the two SCC mix designs and their influence on the
properties of the SCC that is not described in the Chapter 4.3 will shortly be addressed
in this section. The components are: Cement, supplementary cementitious material (fly
ash), aggregates (sand and stone), and the additive air entrainment.
C.1
Cement
The hydraulic binder cement is one of the most important components in any concrete.
Cement is the reason why hardening of concrete occurs and why, how, and at what
speed the strength development of a concrete takes place.
The chemical composition, the fineness of the cement, and the fineness of the surface
of its grains has a significant impact on the quality and property of the fresh SCC.
Very coarse cements will exhibit an increment in risk for bleeding and segregation why
RAPID¬ģ, BASIS¬ģ, or WHITE¬ģ cement from Aalborg Portland would be the natural
choice to use in a SCC cf. [VoSCC (2017)].
The cement used in this Master‚Äôs thesis is a Portland cement of the type RAPID¬ģ CEM
I 52.5N. It is manufactured in a three-stage production process. First the materials are
dug, ground, and suspended. Next clinker is made by burning limestone (about 90%)
and clay together at high temperature (app.
1500¬įC). Finally the clinker is ground
into cement by adding gypsum (about 5% for RAPID¬ģ cement). The gypsum is used
as a set regulator to avoid flash setting (when C3A react with water) [UNSW (2016)].
The raw materials are chalk (Ca), sand (Si), fly ash (Al and Fe), kisash (Fe), and gypsum.
RAPID¬ģ cement can be used in concrete for all purposes and in any environmental
class, why it is the preferable cement used in Ready Mix Concrete concrete. The nota-
tion CEM I means that the cement only consist of pure Portland cement - no limestone
is added. The notation 52.5N means that it has a guaranteed strength of at least 20
MPa after 2 days and 52.5 MPa after 28 days for the cement cf. DS/EN 197-1. The
strength of the concrete will therefore rely on the percentage amount of cement and the
w/cm-ratio used for the concrete [Cement & Beton (2016)] p. 30.
==PAGE:130==
114
C SCC Components
The chemical composition and its influence on the setting time and heat development is
further described in Chapter 3 Section 3.1.1 and Appendix A Section A.2.
C.2
Fly Ash
Supplementary Cementitious Materials (SCM) are a finely grounded material such as
fly ash that can be added to the concrete to improve the properties of the concrete
and/or save the used amount of cement, which have a negative impact on the environ-
ment (high carbon footprint) in the production. Fly ash is an artificial puzzolan from
combustion residues of power plants. It primary consist of glass in the shape of spherical
fines with a proximate same particle size as cement which has a beneficial effect on the
flow properties of the SCC [VoSCC (2017)]. Fly ash itself is not binding but it reacts
with calcium hydroxide and is thereby a soluble product with binding properties. The
chemical puzzolanic reaction is as follows:
SiO2 + 3Ca(OH)2 ‚Üí3CaO ¬∑ 2SiO2 ¬∑ 3H2O
(C.1)
2S + 3CH ‚ÜíC3S2H3(Silicium dioxide + Calcium hydroxide ‚ÜíCSH-gel)
(C.2)
No water is needed for the reaction to take place except for the dissolution. According
to [Beton-Bogen (1985)] p. 295 fly ash contains about 35-55% SiO2 (Silicium dioxide).
This reacts with the calcium hydroxide which is released in the hydration of the cement
(C3S (Alite) and C2S (Belite)).
Reactive SCM like fly ash is characterized by an efficiency factor called the K-factor. The
K-factor is the relationship between the current material‚Äôs contribution to a particular
concrete property in relation to the cement. This factor is crucial for determination of
the w/cm-ratio. The fly ash used in both the mix designs tested in this Master‚Äôs thesis
is of the type called EmiFlyveAske. It is produced by the danish company Emineral A/S
and has a K-factor of 0.5.
Some distinctive features for fresh concrete like SCC containing fly ash is better work-
ability, slower setting, and strength and heat development especially after 28 days of
maturity. The high but very varying amount of unburned charcoal in fly ash makes
it however difficult to control the air contents in the fresh concrete. This is why air
entrainment admixtures is needed in a dose that is 2-4 times higher than the normal
dose. [Aalborg Portland (2017)].
C.3
Aggregates
Aggregates make up the main part of any given concrete due to its low price of which
SCC is no exception (about 60-80% of the concrete volume according to [Mehta &
==PAGE:131==
C.3 Aggregates
115
Monteiro (2014)] p. 241). The used amount of aggregates is normally limited in order
to achieve the design properties needed for the concrete. Since the aggregates constitute
the main part of a concrete it is very important to choose the right composition (fine
vs. coarse), the right shape, and in general the right properties of the aggregates to
achieve a concrete with quality as demanded. For the two SCCs used in this Master‚Äôs
thesis three types of aggregates is used: Sand (0-4 mm), smaller stones (4-8 mm) and
larger stones (8-16 mm) respectively. The most important geometrical, physical, and
chemical properties for the aggregates is listed in Table C.1. Further explanation of the
aggregate‚Äôs geometric characteristics is shortly handled in this section since this subject
have a high influence on the workability of the concrete witch is essential for a high
slump flow and therefore a SCC.
Table C.1: Properties for aggregates
Type

Stone

Geometric characteristics can be split into three categories: Shape, size, and grading.
Shape meaning the form, the angularity, and the surface texture of a particle1. A close-
up of the three types of aggregates can be seen in Figure C.1.
(a) Close up of sand with the
grain size of 0-4 mm
(b) Close up of the smaller
stones with the grain size of 4-
8 mm
(c) Close up of the larger stones
with the grain size of 8-16 mm
Figure C.1: (a) left, (b) center, (c) right. Photos are not to scale.
The sand is a sea material and consists as Figure C.1 (a) shows of a wider and more
uneven group of different particles. All the grains is due to the influence of a long-term
1In Danish the three terms kornform, kantethed & overfladekarakter
==PAGE:132==
116
C SCC Components
attrition on the seabed well-rounded and the consistence of frail particles is as a result
of the attrition very low. The two coarse aggregates (4-8 mm and 8-16 mm stone) that
is shown on Figure C.1 (b) and (c) consists of the same material. Their form is a mix
between cubic and flat and quadratic. Their form (cubic) is very good when it comes
to workability. There is however a risk of water gathering and creating water pockets
between the stone with a flat and quadratic form and the paste which will prevent bind-
ing. The angularity of the coarse aggregates is sharp to round edged2 and prevent a
good workability because of interlocking. The rough surface texture of the stone gives
a higher surface area and therefore very good binding condition for the paste resulting
in a concrete with higher strength properties. A higher surface area is however bad for
the workability of the fresh concrete.
Both of the coarse aggregates are a unfavourable material to use in a SCC (based on
their angularity, and their surface texture) since they prevent good workability for the
fresh SCC. The crushed gneiss/diabase is however the only material that can obey the
demand to be classified as a environmental class E. To make up for the problems the
coarse aggregates causes when it comes to workability, and to create an even better
workability that is seen for an ordinary concrete, additives are used. The used additives
and their features are shortly described in Section C.4.
It is ultimately the composition of all the aggregates which is paramount for the flow
properties, cf. [VoSCC (2017)]. The sand is however the most important part when
it comes to the properties for a SCC. This is caused by sand having a larger span of
particle fraction than the other aggregates why its grain-size distribution curves and
packing ability is crucial for the SCC. The percent distribution of the individual types
of aggregate in relation to the total mass of the aggregates used in this Master‚Äôs thesis
two mix designs is listed in Table C.2. The sand contents for the two mix designs seem
very similar (about 44%), while the contents of 4-8 stone is lower in A35 than in M30
and vice versa for 8-16 stone.
Table C.2: Percent distribution of the individual types of aggregates in relation to the total
mass of the aggregates used in the two mix designs.

A35

The most suitable type of sand for SCC is with rounded grains and with a ‚ÄĚflat‚ÄĚ grain-
size distribution curve where no grain size is dominant cf. [VoSCC (2017)]. Even thought
most of the types of sands have a span in grain size from 0-4 mm, it is common that the
contents of material above 2 mm (some times 1 mm) is very limited which is also the
case for the two SCC mixes in this thesis. For such cases aggregates with a grain size
2In Danish kantrundet.
==PAGE:133==
C.3 Aggregates
117
span of 4-8 mm should be added. To insure stability of the cement paste and mortar it
is also important that the gab between grains from fly ash and cement to the smallest
grain in the sand is limited. This rule can be observed cf. [VoSCC (2017)] by choosing
a type of sand which have a suitable contents of material ‚Č§0.250 mm (15-20%) and
an appropriate amount ‚Č§0.125 mm (4-8%). The grain size distribution for sand, 4-8
stone, 8-16 stone, and the SCC mix A35 and M30 can be seen in Table C.3 and their
corresponding curves in Figure C.2. The table shows that the sand used in this Master‚Äôs
thesis mix designs comply with the recommended values (4% 0.125 mm and 20% 0.25
mm). If this had not been the case adjustments in the mix designs would be needed.
If the sand contained a too low content of fine grains then an increment of the cement
and/or fly ash would have been necessary. This would however entail a lower w/cm-
ratio and therefore a more expensive SCC. A too high content of fine grains would on
the other hand be fatal since this would cause an undesirable fat mortar and therefore
a very bad workability of the SCC.

==PAGE:134==

C SCC Components
C.4
Additives
Chemical additives is added to the mixture so the right properties for the SCC can be
achieved, since this is impossible by only using the ordinary concrete components. The
used additives will shortly be presented in this section.
C.4.1
Air Entrainment
SCC in Denmark is almost always produced with air entrainment.
Air entrainment
admixture is mainly added to the SCC due to the following reasons:
‚ÄĘ The admixture will create and stabilize microscopic (5-300 micrometers) well dis-
tributed spherical shaped air bubbles in the concrete also known as micro air pore
structures. The micro air pore structures will improve the durability of the con-
crete exposed to cycles of freezing and thawing, since the water will be able to
expand in the concrete without causing tension and thus leading to cracks, as illus-
trated in Figure C.3. SCC exposed to freeze/thaw attack should cf. DS/EN 2426
and DS/EN 206 Table F1 have a minimum air content of at least 4.5 volume-%
for a fresh concrete and 3.5 volume-% for hardened concrete.
‚ÄĘ Air is the cheapest filler in concrete. When air is added, the use of the expensive
components is reduced and a cheaper concrete produced. However an increased
amount of air content that is above the natural air content (1-2%) will lead to a
concrete with a significantly reduced compressive strength. According to Aalborg
Portland the strength of the concrete is decreased by 2-5% for every percent of
entrapped air. The durability factor for a concrete will peak when the air content
is ‚Čą7% cf. [El Kordi (2017)] why this should be the upper limit for any type
of concrete (also SCC) with the exception of special concretes such as pervious
concrete.
==PAGE:135==
C.4 Additives
119
Figure C.3: Internal enlarged section of concrete showing the case of freezing. Left: Concrete
without air entrainment admixtures. Here expansion of freezing water leads to concrete cracking.
Right: Concrete with air entrainment admixture. Here air bubbles provide room for freezing
water to expand.
Air entrainment admixtures consist of a long straight organic molecular compound where
each of the molecules is made up by a hydrophilic group in one end and another hydropho-
bic group in the other end. The hydrophilic group entails the substance is dissoluble in
water and the hydrophobic group entails water to get a reduced surface tension which
hereby makes it more easy to mix air into the concrete but also to stabilize the air bub-
bles so they are less likely to turn into to larger pores [Beton-bogen (1985)]. MasterAir
22SB, the air entrainment admixture used in this Master‚Äôs thesis, is made up by sul-
fonates. It is a slight alkaline colorless solution that have to be added the concrete mix
separately and after water have been added but only in a dosage of 0.1-1.5 weight-% of
cement + SCM.
Air entrainment provides a secondary characteristic to the SCC besides the one already
mentioned. Although air generally leads to a risk of separation and bleeding this will
not be a problem when using air entrainment due to the (small) size of the air bubbles.
Instead they will work stabilizing on the paste due to weak stabilizing bonds between
particles and air bubbles [VoSCC (2017)] The micro air pore structures will in addition
to this also increase the workability of the fresh SCC and be waterproofing (imperme-
able) to the hardened SCC cf. the Master‚Äôs thesis supervisor from Aalborg Portland.
==PAGE:136==
120
==PAGE:137==
APPENDIX D
Mixing Procedures in
Laboratory
This appendix is a description of the genetic procedure of mixing SCC at DTU‚Äôs labora-
tory. The procedures from entering the laboratory to casting some of the specimens are
described, while some casting procedures are described in Appendix F. This appendix
describes the following:
‚ÄĘ Safety equipment in the laboratory,
‚ÄĘ Registration of moisture contents in aggregates,
‚ÄĘ Mixing SCC,
‚ÄĘ Testing slump flow of fresh SCC,
‚ÄĘ Testing air entrainment in SCC and mortar.
The casting procedures and execution of other experiments are described in the following
sections in Appendix F:
‚ÄĘ Vicat test measurements using Vicatronic apparatus: Section F.1.1,
‚ÄĘ Proctor penetration testing (incl. casting of specimens): Section F.1.2,
‚ÄĘ Knead bag method procedures: Section F.1.3,
‚ÄĘ Casting and testing of cubes: Section F.2.
D.1
Protection in Laboratory
According to the risks involved in handling and mixing concrete the following precautions
has always been taken: Lab coat and safety boots are worn at all times. Whenever han-
dling either cement or fly ash, mask, eye protection, and gloves are worn. Furthermore
the handling of this is done under air extraction devices when possible. This include
mixing these elements. Chemicals are handled wearing eye protection and gloves. When
the concrete is mixed and casting begins gloves and eye protection are worn. Figure D.1
==PAGE:138==
122
D Mixing Procedures in Laboratory
shows the safety equipment.
Figure D.1: Safety equipment to be worn in the laboratory when mixing concrete.
Prior to entering the Laboratory a chemical risk assessment were filled out and can be
found in Appendix M.
D.2
Registration of Moisture in Aggregates
Before the mix design can be determined it is crucial to know the moisture contents in
the aggregates in order to adjust the amount of water in the SCC. This is done as one of
the first things after arriving at the lab. It is important that the aggregates are stored
inside the lab so they are the same temperature as the room (approximately 20¬įC). The
procedure for determining the moisture in the aggregates are as follows:
‚ÄĘ A petri dish for each aggregate is weighted and the weight written down.
‚ÄĘ A representative specimen of min. 100 g sand, 100 g 4-8 stone, and 350 g 8-16
stone is filled into the petri dishes and weighted once more, see Figure D.2. The
weight is noted. By a representative specimen is meant a specimen that is not
taken from the top of the container. The upper layer may have a more or less
moisture content than the rest of the aggregate.
‚ÄĘ The dishes are put in a microwave oven along with a dish of water. The dish with
water prevents the oven of breaking down. The microwave oven is run at full power
for 5 minutes.
==PAGE:139==
D.2 Registration of Moisture in Aggregates
123
‚ÄĘ After the 5 minutes the oven is opened and the aggregates are stirred to release
potential entrained moisture. Thereafter the oven is once more started and run
for 5 minutes more by full power.
‚ÄĘ The specimens are weighted after it is cooled down to room temperature and the
procedure of 5 min in the oven is repeated until each specimen has a constant
weight.
‚ÄĘ Subtracting the dish‚Äôs weight from the moist and dry aggregates weight, the mois-
ture contents can be found and the mix design adjusted. How the moisture contents
is calculated can be seen in Chapter 3.
Figure D.2: Representative specimens of the three aggregates in petri dishes.
When mix designs are made at Unicon‚Äôs concrete factory in Aved√łre the moisture con-
tent are measured by the factory‚Äôs own automatic probe.
The preliminary experiments showed that the moisture contents in the 0-4 sand might
wary and that a representative speciment might be difficult to obtain. Thus was three
specimens of 0-4 sand selected for the actual experiments. The above procedure was
thus done first with three petri dishes of sand taken from different parts of the stored
piles. The mean moisture contents of the three sand specimens was then entered into
the excel sheet in order to adjust the water in the mix. For the two stone aggregates
only one petri dish was selected according to the above procedure.
==PAGE:140==
124
D Mixing Procedures in Laboratory
D.3
Mixing SCC
All aggregates and additives are stored indoor at approximately 20¬įC. The aggregates
and additives are weighted in separate canisters and readied near the mixer. The follow-
ing instruments and tools are prepared next to the mixer as well:
‚ÄĘ Pressuremeters, big (8 liters volume) and small (1 liter volume), for air entrainment
measurements for SCC and mortar respectively, and for density determination;
‚ÄĘ Buckets and sieves (mesh size 4 and 8 mm) for sieving mortar from the SCC;
‚ÄĘ Slump flow table, cone, rod, and ruler for slump flow test;
‚ÄĘ Proctor penetration instrument, buckets and Testo temperature logger;
‚ÄĘ Cube forms and Testo temperature logger;
‚ÄĘ Bag, bucket, and Testo for the knead bag method.
Instruments and tools for the Vicat measurements are prepared next to the Vicatronic
in the adjecent mortar lab.
Figure D.3 shows the setup just prior to start of mixing. The pressuremeters fulfill the
requirements of DS/EN 196-1 ‚ÄĚMethods of testing cement ‚Äď Part 1: Determination of
strength‚ÄĚ.
Figure D.3: All weighted aggregates and additives are readied near the mixer along with all
tools and instruments. From left: Aggregates, mixer, chemicals and slump cone on slump table,
pressuremeters (small 1 liter and big 8 liters), proctor kit, insulation for box. On the floor:
Buckets with 8 mm sieve and 4 mm sieve, pallet with insulation upon which a bucket with a
plastic bag is along with buckets for the proctor penetration test. Lastly a pallet with the box
with the four forms for three cubes each.
==PAGE:141==
D.3 Mixing SCC
125
Before mixing the SCC, the 200 liter compulsory mixer is damped and excess water is
swept up with paper towels. The SCC is mixed using the following procedure:
‚ÄĘ Larger stones (8-16 mm) are put in the mixer;
‚ÄĘ Smaller stones (4-8 mm) are added;
‚ÄĘ Cement and fly ash is added;
‚ÄĘ Half of the sand is added;
‚ÄĘ The mixer is run for 30 sec;
‚ÄĘ The mixer is stopped and water is added (approximately half a liter of water is
withhold). The time is noted (this is ‚ÄĚzero-time‚ÄĚ);
‚ÄĘ The mixer is run for 30 sec;
‚ÄĘ Through the lid in the mixer the plasticizer (20N) is added while the mixer con-
tinues to run. Part of the withheld water is used to rinse the canister in which
the additive was stored and then poured into the mixer - this ensures that all the
additive is added to the mix;
‚ÄĘ 30 sec after the plasticizer is added the air entrainment (22SB) is added (and its
canister rinsed with the withheld water which is also poured into the mix);
‚ÄĘ After 60 sec1 the superplasticizer (SKY631) is poured through the lid (the rest of
the withheld water is poured into the mixer after rinsing the canister);
‚ÄĘ An additional 30 sec mixing is done before stopping the mixer;
‚ÄĘ The rest of the sand is added and the mixer is run for a final 60 sec, resulting in
a total mixing time of 3 min 30 sec after adding water.
The initial mixing procedure was adding the second half of the sand after the first
dry-mixing and thus before adding water and additives. The preliminary experiments
showed though, that when mixing big quantities (over 100 liters of SCC), it was neces-
sary to add water and additives before the second half of the sand, in order to let the
SCC become more fluent and thus enabling the mixer to turn. The mixing equipment
at DTU did not allow the original mixing procedure due to the big resistance of mixing
approximately a quarter ton dry materials.
Before any tests are done with the freshly mixed SCC, its temperature is measured, see
Figure D.4. A slump flow test can now be performed on the fresh SCC, air entrainment
and density can be determined, proctor penetration experiment and Vicat measurements
1It is important that air entrainment and superplasticizer is not mixed at the same time - thus is
the mixing time between adding these two additives prolonged to 60 sec instead of 30 sec.
==PAGE:142==
126
D Mixing Procedures in Laboratory
can be initiated, casting of cubes can be done, and the bag for the knead bag method
can be filled. For the small pressuremeter and the Vicat measurements mortar is sieved
from the SCC through a 4 mm mesh sieve. For the proctor penetration test the SCC is
sieved through a 8 mm sieve.
Figure D.4: The temperature of the freshly mixed SCC is measured.
To ensure consitency when mixing SCC the template found in Appendix N was followed
every time a SCC was mixed.
D.4
Slump Flow Test
The first thing to do after mixing the SCC is the slump flow test. The test is done ac-
cording to DS/EN 12350-8 ‚ÄĚTesting Fresh Concrete - Part 8: Self-Compacting Concrete
- Slum-Flow Test‚ÄĚ. As opposed to a normal slump test, the slump flow test is used for
SCC. On a table with minimum dimensions of 900 x 900 mm with a smooth flat steel
surface is placed a standard cone (the same as for normal slump tests, but with a 9
kg collar on top). The table is damped and the cone is filled with SCC in a smooth,
homogeneous motion at a constant speed. No compaction by rod is allowed. Surplus
SCC is removed from the top of the cone with the rod, and the table is wiped clean. The
cone is lifted in one smooth vertical movement of approximately 1-3 seconds. When the
SCC has stopped flowing its diameter is measured three different places where it seems
the maximum diameter appears. The measurements are noted and the mean is defined
as the slump flow. Figure D.5 shows the cone just prior to it being lifted (a) and just
after the SCC has stopped flowing (b) and measuring is done.
==PAGE:143==
D.5 Measuring Air Entrainment and Density of Fresh SCC and Mortar
127

with freshly mixed SCC. Surplus SCC is removed
from cone and table.
(b) The cone is lifted and the diameter of the
SCC is measured.

D.5
Measuring Air Entrainment and Density of Fresh
SCC and Mortar
After the SCC is mixed the air entrainment is measured using a pressuremeter (Air
Entrainment Meter). Using a 8 liter pressuremeter the SCC‚Äôs air entrainment is found,
and using a 1 liter pressuremeter the air entrainment of the mortar can be determined.
The following procedure is followed:
‚ÄĘ The buckets of the pressuremeters are weighted and their weights noted.
‚ÄĘ If the air entrainment of the mortar is wished to be determined the SCC is sieved
through a 4 mm mesh so that one liter of mortar is sieved through (see Figure D.6
(a)).
‚ÄĘ The pressuremeters are then filled with 1 liter and 8 liters of mortar and SCC
respectively.
‚ÄĘ The top of the buckets are leveled off with a rod so that the surface of the mor-
tar/SCC is exactly even with the top of the bucket.
==PAGE:144==
128
D Mixing Procedures in Laboratory
‚ÄĘ With a rubber hammer the 8 liter bucket is lightly hit five times on each of its
four sides. This is to release surplus air from the bucket (see Figure D.6 (b)).
‚ÄĘ The edge of the buckets are wiped clean with a wet sponge.
‚ÄĘ The buckets are weighted once more and the weight note. This enables the deter-
mination of the density of the mortar and the SCC.
(a) SCC is sieved through a 4 mm mesh sieve in
order to test the mortar‚Äôs air entrainment.
(b) The filled bucket is lightly hit with a rubber
hammer in order to release surplus air prior
to testing.
Figure D.6: (a) left, (b) right.
‚ÄĘ The lids are placed on top of the buckets with the valves open and locked onto
them.
‚ÄĘ Demineralized boiled water is poured into one valve until it spills out of the other
(see Figure D.7 (a)). The pressuremeter is lightly hit on the side to ensure all air
can escape the container.
‚ÄĘ The valve from which the water is poured into is closed first and then the other.
‚ÄĘ The pumping handle on top of the pressuremeter is worked until the hand of the
display is above the red marker.
‚ÄĘ The hand is adjusted so that it is precisely above the red marker by pressing the
black button (see Figure D.7 (b)). Lightly hit the display of the pressuremeter to
ensure the hand is not stuck.
‚ÄĘ The green button is pressed and hold until the hand stands still. The measured
air entrainment is written down. Note that the pressure might fall a bit right after
the button is released. The highest measured air entrainment is the correct.
==PAGE:145==

APPENDIX E
Preliminary Experiments
This appendix describes in detail the lessons learned during the preliminary experiments.
The essence has been summarized in Section 6.1.5.
E.0.1
P.E. #1
The first preliminary experiment was mainly testing the Vicatronic automatic Vicat
Needle Test apparatus. A mortar (disregarding additives and stone aggregates) was
poured into a plastic mould of no specific dimensions and was not submerged into water
as the standard DS 196-3 prescribes. The functionality and operational procedures were
familiarized.
E.0.2
P.E. #2
The second preliminary experiment still only concerned the Viatronic, but was conducted
as prescribed in the standard DS 196-3, i.e. the specimen was poured into a cone-shaped
hard plastic form and submerged into temperate demineralized water. Further more
additives were added to the mix, but still excluding stone. There was thus not followed
a specific recipe. As Figure E.1 shows test intervals of 10 min resulted in a quite steep
curve of the setting development, and intervals of 5 min were decided to be tested in the
next preliminary experiment.

In order to get a

penetrations).
Note that Figure E.2 shows both the actual plot (dotted line) and the so-called adjusted
more correct plot (fully drawn line).
The adjusted plot is based on the assumption
that the mortar does not become less set during time. Thus can the needle only be
less penetrated through the mortar for each penetration. The reason why the needle
sometimes show higher values and then penetrate deeper next time, must be because
the needle is obstructed by sand grains. This assumption and decision to depict the test
results like this was taken in collaboration with the supervisors from Aalborg Portland
who are familiar with the Vicat Needle Test and experts regarding setting time of mortars
and pastes.

Still only mixing a small amount of mortar (no stones added in the mix) it was experi-
enced that adding the correct amount of additives was quite difficult since it regarded
so small amounts. The mix‚Äôs air entrainment was measured using pressuremeters (Air
Entrainment Meters) and the density calculates as well. Based on the experience of P.E.
==PAGE:149==

133
#3 regarding the detail of the setting time, 10 min. test intervals were chosen once
again. It was also experienced that it was difficult to add the correct amount of water
when stones were omitted. The mix was not correct and it was decided to the future
preliminary experiments with complete SCCs.
E.0.5
P.E. #5
The first large amount of SCC was mixed with all aggregates and additives. Aggregates
and water was not temperate though.
The Vicat test showed wrong results due to
the cone form lifting itself from the glass plate.
This was avoided for the following
experiments by pressing the form down while pouring the mortar into the form. Cubes
were cast in order to initiate preliminary experiments with the compressive strength
machines. These cubes were cast without any kind of vibration and just by shoveling
the SCC into the forms (were not dropped from any height). Sawing a cube in half
showed many air bubles cast in and the surface clearly showed entrapped air leaving the
cube after casting, see Figure E.3.
Figure E.3: Cubes which were not exposed to any kind of vibration or compaction.
It was decided to hit the forms five times on each side with a rubber hammer in order
to let entrapped air escape. This only imitates the real process of casting SCC where it
either will be pumped or dropped from a height corresponding to the applied force of
the rubber hammer.

cubes to test. However the amount of water to add to the mix design was miscalculated
(due to flaws in the excel sheet) and the batch was discarded.

E.0.8
P.E. #8
Preliminary experiment number eight was supposed to be the first full-scale experiment
where a calculated amount of 160 l SCC was to be mixed at one time. The largest mixer
at DTU supposedly holds 200 l but the mixer was not able to dry-mix the large amount
of aggregates, cement, and fly ash. As a consequence the amount was decided to be
decreased to 120 l. Instead of casting three buckets for the Proctor Penetration Tests
only two was decided upon.
E.0.9
P.E. #9
Reducing the total amount of one SCC batch to 120 l the mixer was able to dry-mix
before adding water and additives to the mix. Since the water and additives makes the
mix fluid it was decided to only fill half of the sand in the mixer, then adding water and
additives, and then the rest of the sand. This would allow the fresh SCC to become flu-
ent, and thus ease the mixing for the machine. The mixer was thus filled in the following
order: Larger stones, smaller stones, half of the sand, cement, and fly ash, dry-mixing,
adding water and additives, then the rest of the sand, and then a final mixing. When
adding the additives it proved difficult to ensure all additives was added and none left in
the canisters in which they had been weighted. It was thus decided to withhold a small
amount of the water from the mix, and then using this water to rinse the canisters of
the additives so as to ensure all additives would be mixed into the SCC.
P.E. #9 also showed that when the early compressive strength of the cubes were to be
tested the TONI 3000 machine was not fitting. The TONI has a maximum load of 3000
==PAGE:152==
136
E Preliminary Experiments
kN and the cubes has to be applied a load of approximately 7 kN at the earliest stage
of testing, why the TONI was not able to measure the relative small failure load. It
was thus decided to use the Instron 6025 machine with a maximum load of 100 kN and
thus a better ability to measure the relative small failure loads. For the eight first cubes
the Instron would be used, for the 1 day of maturity and 28 days of maturity cubes
(#9-#10 and #11-#12 respectively) the TONI would still be used. Figure E.7 shows
the difference in measured compressive strength for two similar SCC batches (P.E. #9
and E. 1) using the TONI and the Instron respectively.

E.0.10
P.E. #10
The mixing procedure was decided to be adjusted one last time based on the experiences
made from preliminary experiment number nine. When the dry-mixing commenced a
significant amount of the cement and fly ash spilled over the top of the mixer. This was
due to the light weight of the cement and fly ash, and it was thus decided to fill the
mixer in the following order: Larger stone, smaller stone, cement, fly ash, half of the
sand, dry-mixing, water, additives, and finally the rest of the sand.

APPENDIX F
Testing Procedures in
Laboratory
The following appendix describes in detail how the different experiments are conducted.
Each test method is shortly presented and its relevance explained, but otherwise this
appendix focuses on the procedures, and thus enables the reader of this masters thesis to
replicate the experiments and to assure the quality of the performed tests. For preceding
procedures (mixing SCC) see Appendix D.
F.1
Setting time
Three different test methods are used to determine the setting time of the two SCC
recipes. Each has its own practical pros and cons, each has its own relevance in differ-
ent stages of the setting development (the hardening of the SCC). The first and most
scientific precise is the Vicat Test: Using a Vicatronic automatic setting time appara-
tus any number of tests can be done exactly the same way with a minimum of human
interference. The second is the Proctor Penetration Test witch is time consuming for
the user but gives a good picture of the hardening process. The final is the Knead Bag
Method which is most applicable in the field, i.e. on the construction site, but is the
most unscientific precise since it is up to the individual user to assess the different stages
of the hardening process. Any comparison of the different methods will not be discussed
in this appendix but in Chapter 6.
F.1.1
Vicat Test
The determination of the setting time using a Vicatronic is described in this section
and is based on the standard DS/EN 196-3:2016 ‚ÄĚMethods of Testing Cement ‚Äď Part
3: Determination of Setting Times and Soundness‚ÄĚ. The setting time (initial and fi-
nal) is determined by observing the penetration of a needle in a cement paste with a
standard consistency, until the penetration of the needle have reached a specific depth.
It is important to notice that the setting time is investigated with an automatic Vicat
apparatus and not the manual one.
==PAGE:156==
140
F Testing Procedures in Laboratory
DS/EN 196-3:2016 is based on setting time for cement paste, whereas the standard
DS/EN 480-2:2006 ‚ÄĚAdmixtures for Concrete, Mortar and Grout ‚Äď Test Methods ‚Äď Part
2: Determination of Setting Time‚ÄĚ is based on setting times for concrete and mortars
containing admixtures which intuitively would have been more correct to use for the
concrete recipe investigated in this masters thesis. The choice of using 196-3:2016 is
made in collaboration with the supervisors from Aalborg Portland and is based on the
lack of experience with DS/EN 480-2:2006 and a desire to be able to see the correlation
between cement, mortar and concrete. The differences between the two standards is
described in Subsection F.1.1.3.
A couple of deviations from the norm DS/EN 196-
3:2016 have been necessary. These can be found in Subsection F.1.1.2.
F.1.1.1
Execution
The laboratory facility where the test is carried out is maintained at a temperature of 20.0
¬Ī 2.0¬įC and with a relative humidity RH ‚Č•50% at all time. This demand is controlled
before, during and after the test. The apparatus used to test the specimens and the
water in the container, which is recorded during the test, needs to have a temperature
of 20.0 ¬Ī 2.0¬įC as well to ensure a curing where 1 hour equals 1 maturity hour and to
avoid dehydration of the sample. The Vicat apparatus‚Äôs moving parts needs to have a
total mass of 300 ¬Ī 1 gram and their movement have to be truly vertical and happen
without substantial friction. The necessary equipment used to perform the experiment
is shown on Figure F.1.
Figure F.1: Equipment used to execute the Vicatronic tests. In front of the Vicatronic is from
left to rigth: A bucket, a rubber ring, a glass plate, and the special Vicat mould.
==PAGE:157==
F.1 Setting time
141
Calibration of Vicatronic Before Tests:
A calibration of the Vicatronic is necessary before starting the test. This is done in
order to insure credible results from the Vicatronic since each setup might vary. The
calibration is done prior to every test. The calibration is carried out as follows:
1. A Vicat mould made by hard plastic with a truncated conical shape with a internal
diameter of 75¬Ī10 mm and a height of 40¬Ī0.2 mm is placed on top of a plane glass
plate with a height of at least 2.5 mm and a diameter bigger than the mould. Both
the mould and the glass plate is placed on a rubber ring in a container which is
bigger than the setup. The rubber ring is to create a non-slip connection between
the glass plate and the container. The setup with the stated dimension of the used
items is shown in Figure F.2
(a) Cross-section of the mould-setup for
the Vicatronic test with the corresponding
measurements.
(b) Mould setup for the Vicatronic.
Figure F.2: (a) left, (b) right.
2. The needle attached to the Vicatronic apparatus (made of steel and in the form
of a cylinder with a diameter of 1.13¬Ī0.05 mm and a effective length of at least
45 mm) is pulled up carefully and the mould-setup is placed on the Vicatronic
platform as shown in Figure F.3.
3. The following steps are done on the Vicatronic‚Äôs interface by using the maneuver
button - to navigate scroll the maneuver button, press the button to pick a category.
Use the button ‚ÄĚEsc‚ÄĚ to undo your choice:
Instrument -> Needle calibration -> Calibration at 0 mm.

Automatic Vicat Tests:
Two identical Vicat moulds is then filled.
One is for the Vicatronic, the other is a
reference sample for registration of temperature development. A thermal logger cord
is cast into the reference sample to record the temperature development. Likewise a
thermal logger cord is placed in the container with the water and in the room to log
the temperature during the whole experiment. The samples are readied in the following
way:
1. The SCC is mixed and the mortar sieved in accordance with Section D.3. The
sieved mortar is filled in the moulds as soon as possible.
2. The mould is filled to the top (40 mm) without compaction or vibration. Any
voids in the paste is removed by gently tapping the mould against the ball of the
hand. It is ensured that the upper surface of the paste is smooth after this leveling
and tapping.
3. The mould is immersed in a container. This container is filled with demineralized
water with a temperature of 20.0¬Ī2.0¬įC to a level that is at least 5 mm higher
than the surface of the paste, see figure F.6 (a).
4. The needle is carefully pulled up on the apparatus and the mould, container, and
base-plate (glass plate) placed centralized at the Vicatronic platform. The needle
is released slowly and it is checked that the tip of needle is not dipped in the paste,
see Figure F.6 (b).
5. The settings for the Vicat apparatus is set up using the interface:
In the menu ‚ÄĚExecution‚ÄĚ -> ‚ÄĚKind of test‚ÄĚ the test method ‚ÄĚEN 196-3-2005‚ÄĚ is
chosen. This means that the Vicatronic will run the test as described in DS/EN
196-3 (no change regarding this procedure between editions 2005 and 2016) and
perform a total number of 86 penetrations in a profile identical to Figure F.5. The
penetration profiles fulfill the requirements; A minimum distance of 8 mm between
a penetration and the rim of the mould and at least 5 mm distance between each
penetration in which a distance of at least 10 mm from the last penetration position
is needed.
==PAGE:160==
144
F Testing Procedures in Laboratory
Figure F.5: Test profile for penetration pattern according to DS/EN 196-3:2016.

is set to ‚ÄĚNO‚ÄĚ.
7. As ‚ÄĚFixed Time‚ÄĚ ‚ÄĚ9 min‚ÄĚ is chosen and ‚ÄĚStart‚ÄĚ is pressed1.
8. The test will run for 774 minutes (12 hours and 54 minutes). When the test is
done the needle is pulled up and the mould, container, and base-plate removed
from the platform (Figure F.6 (c)).

The extraction of the data is done by making a print from the Vicat apparatus as

1. Select ‚ÄĚFile test‚ÄĚ.

3. Choose ‚ÄĚComplete print‚ÄĚ.
The test results will be printed with each measurement (line 1-86) and corresponding
penetration measured as the distance from the bottom to the top (0 mm corresponding
to the needle hitting the bottom glass plate and 40 mm being no penetration due to
complete hardening of the sample). Figure F.7 shows the printing of results via the
Vicatronic.
Figure F.7: Printing Vicat results directly from the Vicatronic.
Determination of Initial and Final Setting:
The definition of initial setting using this method is the time elapsed between ‚ÄĚzero time‚ÄĚ
(when adding water during the mixing procedures) to when the needle is penetrating to
a depth of 6¬Ī3 mm from the base-plate measured to the nearest minute. The definition
of final setting is the time to the nearest minute elapsed from ‚ÄĚzero time‚ÄĚ to when the
needle only penetrates 0.5 mm into the specimen, meaning a distance from the surface
and into the paste.
==PAGE:162==
146
F Testing Procedures in Laboratory
F.1.1.2
Deviations from the Norm
Deviation from the standard have been necessary due to limitation in the laboratory at
DTU. Primarily the ability to completely control the temperature of the room in which
the Vicatronic is placed were not possible. According to the norm the room temperature
has to be measured once before the test commences. This might show the requirements
for the temperature is respected, but when the data is examined after the test is over,
the logging from the reference test might show that the temperature in the room has
exceeded or fallen below the requirements with a few degrees Celsius. By logging the
temperature development in the reference sample, the water, and the air, the minor
deviations can be monitored. Furthermore the exact temperature is used to calculate
the maturity of the specimen why any deviation from the norm not will have any effect
on the test.
Another deviation is the lack of a mineral-based oil as formwork oil. The norm mentions
that some oils have been found to affect the Vicat test, but using mineral-based oils
prevents this. Lacking mineral-based oils it has been chosen to not use formwork oil for
the Vicat tests.

F.1.2
Proctor Penetration Test
The determination of the setting time using Proctor penetration is described in this
chapter and is based on the standard DS 423.17 from 1984. Although the method is
more than 30 years old and pretty time-consuming it is still the preferred method used
at most concrete factories and construction sites in Denmark. This is mainly due to its
very simple and reasonably accurate method of execution. The setting time, meaning
the strength development during the preliminary hardening, is determined by observing
the resistance when penetrating different sized plungers (from the specific proctor kit)
into an SCC without larger aggregates (>8mm) at different times. Unlike the method
described in Subsection F.1.1, this method only facilitate determination of initial setting
defined as a concrete with a penetration resistance of 3.5 MPa (must take place less than
8 hours after mixing)2. The concrete cannot be tested if it has a fully or partial content of
lightweight aggregate. Likewise the biggest granular of the aggregates Dmax needs to be
less than 8 mm. The Proctor penetrometer used in this masters thesis is a Matest S088
Proctor Penetrometer with a spring load scale of 0-100 kg (subdiv. 1 kg), with direct
max. value reading in kilograms on the sliding rod. Five interchangeable cylindrical
plungers made of stainless steel with a pressure surface of 32 mm2 (D=6.4 mm, ‚ÄĚplunger
No. 1‚ÄĚ), 65 mm2 (D=9.0 mm, ‚ÄĚplunger No. 2‚ÄĚ), 160 mm2 (D=14.3 mm, ‚ÄĚplunger No.
3‚ÄĚ), 325 mm2 (D=20.3 mm, ‚ÄĚplunger No. 4‚ÄĚ) and 650 mm2 (D=28.8 mm, ‚ÄĚplunger
No. 5‚ÄĚ) and a height of at least 25 mm can be mounted at the end of the Proctor
penetrometer and is used to do the test. The complete proctor kit can be seen in Figure
F.8.
Figure F.8: Proctor kit used to execute the penetration tests.
2Final setting is also determined based on the results of the Proctor Penetration Test but using
assumptions from the American ASTM C403 described in Chapter 7
==PAGE:164==
148
F Testing Procedures in Laboratory
The standard mentions plungers of the sizes 50, 100, 200, 400, and 800 mm2, but the
kit at hand had the previous mentioned sizes which then was used. It is assessed that
the different plunger sizes does not affect the test since the applied force is divided by
the plunger area in order to get the strength of the SCC. Tabel F.2 shows the plungers
from the standard and the ones used.

F.1.2.1
Execution
‚ÄĘ Aggregate grains bigger than 8 mm is separated from the freshly mixed SCC
according to Section D.3 using a sieve with a mesh size of 8 mm, see Figure F.9
(a).
‚ÄĘ The concrete is poured into two containers with an internal diameter of at least
150 mm and a depth of 120-150 mm. Two containers are needed so simultaneous
tests can be done to ensure trustworthy results. The dense containers is made of
hard reinforced plastic (masonry buckets) that proof the chemistry of the concrete
and stay rigid during the test. The buckets are depicted in Figure F.9 (b).
‚ÄĘ A thermo thread is placed in the middle of the concrete in each buckets and the
temperature is logged during the whole test, see Figure F.9 (c).
‚ÄĘ With a 30 min interval starting from the ‚ÄĚzero-time‚ÄĚ (when water was added in
the mixing process) a finger is penetrated into the surface of the SCC in every
bucket. When the SCC begins to create some resistance against the finger it is
time to start using the proctor penetrometer, see Figure F.9 (d).
‚ÄĘ The biggest plunger, plunger No. 5, is mounted on the proctor penetrometer. The
plunger is pressed 25 mm into the concrete surface in each of the buckets with
a force that is directed perpendicular to the SCC surface and with a constant
speed so that the penetration takes approximately 10 seconds. If the height of the
plunger exceed 25 mm it is important to keep the penetration depth at 25 mm.
The force used to press the plunger into the SCC is read off the sliding rod, see
Figure F.9 (e).
‚ÄĘ Using a testing interval of 15 min this procedure is continued. Every time a force
higher than 20 kg is used to penetrate the SCC, the plunger is replaced with one
==PAGE:165==
F.1 Setting time
149
having a smaller diameter (lower No.)3. To avoid earlier penetration or that the
container impacts the results, each penetration is done at least 25 mm from another
penetration hole or from the bucket wall, see Figure F.9 (f).
‚ÄĘ The test is completed when a force equal to 5 MPa is used for penetrating the
concrete in all buckets.

F.1.3
The Knead Bag Method
A simple and practical method of testing the setting time of SCC is the so-called knead
bag method. Described in the German Standard DIN 18218:2010-01 ‚ÄĚPressure of Fresh
Concrete on Vertical Formwork‚ÄĚ Appendix A the method provides a slightly unscientific
but very applicable procedure described in this section. Depending on the users percep-
tion of applied pressure and assessment of the SCC texture the method is not completely
objective. That being said the high applicability and widespread use of the knead bag
method makes it interesting to compare with other setting time determination methods.
F.1.3.1
Equipment
The following equipment is needed for the knead bag method:
‚ÄĘ A sealable plastic bag with a volume of at least 20 liters,
‚ÄĘ A scoop for the fresh SCC,
‚ÄĘ A bucket with a volume of at least 10 liters and an internal diameter of 25 to 30
cm,
‚ÄĘ A Testo temperature logger,
==PAGE:168==
152
F Testing Procedures in Laboratory
F.1.3.2
Test Procedure
Directly after mixing the SCC, the sampling is carried out as follows: A representable
sample of approximately 8 liters SCC is placed in the bag which is placed in the bucket.
The bucket is placed on an insulated slab. A thermo thread is placed in the middle of
the concrete, the bag sealed as tight as possible around the thread, and the temperature
is measured and noted, see Figure F.12.
Figure F.12: Setup of the knead bag method.
Every minute the temperature is logged. When the SCC is of ‚ÄĚplastic‚ÄĚ to ‚ÄĚstiff‚ÄĚ con-
sistency (in accordance with Table F.4) the bag is taken out of the bucket and placed
directly on the insulating slab. Every 30 min. thereafter a finger is pressed into the side
of the bag with a ‚ÄĚmedium‚ÄĚ force of approximately 50 N or 5 kg. Every time the finger is
pressed into the bag it has to be a new place. When the fresh SCC is of ‚ÄĚset‚ÄĚ consistency
(in accordance with Table F.4) the test is done and the end of setting, tE,knead, is noted.
Intervals may be increased when the end of setting is considered imminent. The final
setting is defined as tE = tE,knead ¬∑ 1.25.
==PAGE:169==
F.2 Compressive Strength
153
Table F.4: Description of the setting condition of SCC using the knead bag method
Consistency:
Description:
Fluid
Flows in the bag
Soft
Does not flow, oozes when pressed
Plastic
Kneadable
Stiff
Can be indented to a max. depth of 30 mm
Semi-firm
Can be indented to a max. depth of 10 mm
Set
Can be indented to a depth of less than 1.0 mm
F.1.3.3
Deviations form the Norm
Temperature is measured in the middle of the SCC and not on the surface of the bag as
described in the norm. Instead of only measuring every 30 min, measuring intervals are
increased when nearing the expected final setting time in order to obtain a more precise
final setting time.
F.2
Compressive Strength
In order to investigate the correlation between maturity and compressive strength, SCC
cubes of 150 x 150 x 150 mm are cast. By casting in thermo treads in selected cubes, the
heat development and thus the maturity of the SCC can be calculated, and the cubes can
be tested for their compressive strength at different maturity hours. The testing is done
in either a TONI 3000 or a Instron 5500R testing apparatus. The machines are placed in
the basement and ground floor respectively of building 119 at DTU Lyngby Campus and
do both fulfill the requirements according to DS/EN 12390-4:2000 ‚ÄĚTesting Hardened
Concrete ‚Äď Part 4: Compressive Strength ‚Äď Specification for Testing Machines‚ÄĚ.
F.2.1
Casting SCC Cubes
Prior to mixing the SCC the forms for the 150 x 150 x 150 mm cubes are prepared. A
thin layer of form oil is applied on the inside of the formwork in order to prevent the
SCC from sticking to it. The temperature logger is prepared by plugging in thermo
threads into the four channels. For three of the four forms a thermo tread is cast in the
center of one of the cubes. The fourth thermo thread is for the air temperature inside
the insulated box (see Figure F.13). The forms are lightly hit with a rubber hammer five
times on two sides for every cube. This is in order to release entrapped air and prevent
air bubbles in the surface of the cubes.
==PAGE:170==
154
F Testing Procedures in Laboratory
Figure F.13: Setup of forms for cube castings.
When the SCC is mixed it is poured into the forms with a spoon. The SCC is dropped
into the forms from app. 30 cm height in order to release excess air. For the cubes where
a thermo thread must be cast in, the thread is held in place while the SCC is poured
slowly into the form, see Figures F.14 (a) and (b). When the forms are full the top is
leveled off with the hand by making a movement that resembles ‚ÄĚjutning‚ÄĚ so that the
cubes‚Äô tops are even. The forms are then placed in the insulated box (see Figure F.15)
and the lid closed.
(a) The fresh SCC is poured into the forms. A
thermo thread is held in place to be cast in.
(b) The forms are leveled off with a hand.
Figure F.14: (a) left, (b) right.
==PAGE:171==
F.2 Compressive Strength
155
Figure F.15: The cubes have been cast, thermo threads are cast in selected cubes, and the
insulated box can be closed.
F.2.2
Preparation of Cubes
When a cube is ready for compressive strength testing it is carefully taken out of its
form (the early cubes are soft and easily injured). The cube is then photographed from
to diagonally opposite angles so that all six sides of the cubes are documented. Figure
F.16 shows how the cubes are documented. All photos can be found on the attached
USB flash drive. The measurements l1 and l2 are a surface perpendicular to the top side
during casting. In the figure side A would have been facing upwards during casting. The
side l1 x l2 (B) is the surface that is going to face the pressure plates in the compressive
strength machine. The lengths l1 and l2 are measured with a vernier caliper and the
area l1 x l2 used to calculate the compressive strength of the cube, see Section A.3.
Figure F.16: Sketch showing how photos have been taken of all cubes prior to testing. The
measurements l1 x l2 (side B) is the side of the cube facing the upper pressure plate in the
compressive strength machine.
==PAGE:172==
156
F Testing Procedures in Laboratory
F.2.3
Procedure for Compressive Strength Testing Using TONI 3000
The 150 x 150 x 150 mm SCC cubes can be tested in the TONI 3000 testing machine
which setup can be seen in Figure F.17. This machine is capable to enforce a force of
3000 kN and is ideal for testing high compressive strengths. The preliminary experiments
showed that the TONI 3000 might not register the relative low failure load applied (lower
than 100 kN), why only cubes #9 - #12 (24 hour old cubes and cubes of 28 days of
maturity) were tested in the TONI 3000. When the TONI 3000 machine were used the
following procedure were followed:
Figure F.17: Setup of TONI 3000 compressive strength machine.
1. The surfaces of the two pressure plates are cleaned with a broom and if necessary
a scraper.
2. The specimen is placed centralized on the pressure plates perpendicular to the
direction of casting.
If necessary a steel block is placed centralized under the
specimen to ensure a proper height according to the upper pressure plate (the
piston will not press the pressure plates closer to each other than 80 mm). A ruler
is used to ensure the specimen is centralized on the pressure plate, see Figure F.18
(a).
3. When the specimen is placed the safety shield is placed in front of the load frame.
4. The temperature of the cooler connected to the machine is checked. If the temper-
ature is above 50¬įC the machine may not be used.
==PAGE:173==
F.2 Compressive Strength
157
5. The machine is turned on by switching the safety knob and the then the key. The
display will turn on.
6. ‚ÄĚSTART‚ÄĚ is pressed to enter the menu ‚ÄĚPARM‚ÄĚ for parameters.
7. The menu is navigated with the arrow buttons and the following parameters are
chosen:
‚ÄĘ ‚ÄĚTest type: Compress‚ÄĚ because compressive strength testing is wished,
‚ÄĘ ‚ÄĚControl: F = LOAD‚ÄĚ to instruct the machine to be controlled by force,
‚ÄĘ ‚ÄĚDisp: F [kN]‚ÄĚ to make sure the output is measured in [kN].
8. ‚ÄĚSTART‚ÄĚ is pressed to continue.
9. The dimensions of the specimen is set to ‚ÄĚd [mm] = 150.00‚ÄĚ and ‚ÄĚh [mm] = 150.00‚ÄĚ
(according to the cube‚Äôs dimensions).
10. ‚ÄĚdF/dt‚ÄĚ is set according to which cubes are to be tested. Cubes #9 and #10 are
tested with a force corresponding to that of the Instron test machine, see Section
F.2.4. Cubes #11 and #12 are tested with a speed according to DS/EN 12390-3
‚ÄĚTesting Hardened Concrete ‚Äď Part 3: Compressive Strength of Test Specimens‚ÄĚ.
‚ÄĘ For cubes #9 and #10 what are to be tested 24 hours after adding water,
the speed of the TONI is set to 1.5 kN/s corresponding to 90 kN/min which
cubes #1-#8 is tested with in the Instron machine. This speed is determined
from experience with the preliminary tests so that the cubes will have a test
time of approximately 30 sec before failure. In the TONI display the speed
is thus set to ‚ÄĚdF/dt = 01.50‚ÄĚ.
‚ÄĘ For cubes #11 and #12 which are tested after 28 days of maturity, the speed
is set to 13.5 kN/s corresponding to a speed of 0.6¬Ī0.2 MPa/s according to
DS/EN 12390-3.
11. ‚ÄĚFRACT.DET: LOAD [%]:‚ÄĚ is set to ‚ÄĚ05.000‚ÄĚ as an initial setting. This means
the machine will continue the test 5% after the failure occurs. If a more clear
failure crack is wished the setting can be increased, or opposite if the specimen is
too much destroyed the setting can be decreased.
12. ‚ÄĚSTART‚ÄĚ is pressed to confirm.
The display will show your entered settings.
‚ÄĚSTART‚ÄĚ is pressed once more to confirm.
The machine will start (the noise
is obvious).
13. The pressure plate will adjust according to the specimen, and when ready (the
display says so) ‚ÄĚSTART‚ÄĚ is pressed to initiate the test. The start-button will
turn green: The test is running.
14. When the failure occurs the machine automatically stops.
The failure load is
displayed. It is written down and 4 kN is subtracted (the zero-display value).
==PAGE:174==
158
F Testing Procedures in Laboratory
15. After noting the failure load ‚ÄĚSTART‚ÄĚ is pressed once more. The machine will
lower the piston so the specimen can be removed. The safety shield is removed
and the destroyed specimen thereafter. Figure F.18 (b) shows a cube after testing.
16. The upper and lower pressure plates are cleaned, and the tests are repeated with
other specimens from number 13 and onward.
17. When the last test is done, ‚ÄĚSTOP‚ÄĚ is pressed. The machine will lower the piston
completely. By pressing ‚ÄĚSTOP‚ÄĚ once more the machine turns off.
18. The last specimen is removed and the pressure plates are cleaned.
19. The machine is turned off by turning the key subsequently the safety knob.
(a) Centering of cube prior to compres-
sive strength testing.
(b) The SCC cube after testing.
Figure F.18: (a) left, (b) right.
F.2.4
Procedure of Compressive Strength Testing Using Instron
5500R
As a consequence of the low failure loads required to break the cubes, and the TONI
3000‚Äôs disability to register the relative small loads, an Instron 5500R testing machine
were used insted for the cubes to be tested at early stages. The Instron machine has
a maximum load of 100 kN whereas the TONI has a maximum load of 3000 kN. The
==PAGE:175==
F.2 Compressive Strength
159
lower maximum load of the Instron machine also makes it better at registering the low
failure loads.
F.2.4.1
Setup of Merlin Data Logger Software
Connected to the Instron testing machine is a PC with the software ‚ÄĚMerlin‚ÄĚ which both
sets the properties of the Instron, initiates the testing, and registers the applied load
and displacement of the crosshead. The Merlin software is setup in the following way:
‚ÄĘ Open the Merlin software located on the desktop of the PC.
‚ÄĘ If asked in a dialog box choose ‚ÄĚBrick Tension‚ÄĚ.
‚ÄĘ If the experiment has been conducted before, choose ‚ÄĚFile/Open Method‚ÄĚ and
choose your setup file (e.g. ‚ÄĚsetup.mtA‚ÄĚ).
‚ÄĘ Check under ‚ÄĚFile/Setup‚ÄĚ if your destination folder is chosen, and if ‚ÄĚASCII Raw
Data to:‚ÄĚ is checked (and the others are not). See Figure F.19.
Figure F.19: Merlin Data Logger Software: Window for destination folder selection and setup
file.
‚ÄĘ Open ‚ÄĚTest Control‚ÄĚ in the right side of the screen (the traffic light icon) and make
sure the settings are as seen in Figure F.20.

F.2.4.2
Setup for Compressive Strength Testing with Instron 5500R
When the Merlin software is setup according to previous section the test can be carried
out in the following way:
‚ÄĘ The Instron 5500R is set up according to Figure F.23.
‚ÄĘ The specimen is placed centralized on a thick solid steel plate and subsequently
placed on the circular pressure plate of the Instron. On top of the specimen an
additional steel plate is placed as well. This is to ensure an even pressure from the
circular pressure plates onto the square cube. Figure F.24 (a) shows the cube in
the Instron prior to testing.
‚ÄĘ The crosshead is manually moved to the upper pressure plate. Make shure it does
not touch the upper pressure plate though.
‚ÄĘ The ‚ÄĚBalance Load‚ÄĚ and ‚ÄĚReset Gauge Length‚ÄĚ can be pressed and thereafter the
‚ÄĚStart Test‚ÄĚ.
‚ÄĘ Figure F.24 (b) shows a cube after testing.

G.4
Results from Laboratory Experiments
The following section lists all results from the laboratory experiments (denoted E1 to
E6). The figures show among other things how the results have been adjusted to take
maturity into account, convert compressive strengths of cubes to comparative cylinder
strengths, and other fittings and adjustments. The adjustments are described in Chapter
7 but all figures are in this appendix whereas only the most important can be found in
Chapter 7.
G.4.1
Measured Slump Flow, Air Contents, Density, and
Temperatures
The slump flow of all mixes have been determined prior testing the SCC. A slump flow
measurement have been carried out just after mixing the concrete and in the end of
casting (corresponding to a time range of 56-69 minutes). The measured slump flow can
be seen in Figure G.1. In general it has been observed that the slump flow will decline
over time. A fresh SCC with a very high slump flow (>520 mm) will decrease with a
major value compared to a fresh SCC with a lower slump flow (<520 mm). For mixes
with very low slump flow no reduction was seen over a time range of about 60 min. In
general all mixes varies a lot in relation to the designed slump flow of 570 mm, which is
==PAGE:186==
170
G Results
a known problem according to the supervisor from Unicon - also at the batching plants.
The varying slump flow seen for the seven mixtures will however not have an significant
impact on the setting time according to the supervisor from Aalborg Portland.

==PAGE:187==
G.4 Results from Laboratory Experiments
171
In general it have been observed that the air content for all SCC mixes was below the
design air content of 6.5%. However the air content is not crucial for the time of setting
but only the workability and the ability to resist freeze/thaw attack. The air content
for the mortar are for all the mixes higher than the one determined for the SCC. This
make sense since the mortar contains a higher percentage of pasta in which the air occurs.
The density of both the fresh SCC and mortar have been determined of all the mixes
like the air content. However the density of the mixes used for the formwork pressure
experiment was only determined at the factory since no scale was brought along to
the site. The determined density and the strived density (black lines) for the different
mixtures can be seen in Figure G.3. The figure shows that the actual density for all
the mixes are higher than the strived density. Since the strived density is calculated
according to a air content of 6.5% and the actual air content have been proven to be
smaller a higher density was thus expected. The density of the sieved mortar are about
150-200 kg/m3 lower than the SCC. Since the aggregates is the component in concrete
with the highest density sieving a percentage of them away would therefore results in a
lower density.

119 at DTU) whereas the batching plant mixed the concrete outdoors on a warm summer
day.

G.4.5
Cubes Experiments

experiments eight cubes were tested as early as possible with 30 min intervals in order
to identify the early strength development. The last four cubes of each experiment was
tested after 1 day of maturity and 28 days of maturity (two cubes at each time). Figure
G.18 show the compressive strength from the first eight cubes of experiment 1-3 (E1-E3)
with the A35 SCC before and after adjustment for maturity.

This section shows how photo documentation of the 160 cubes were done just after
removing the formwork and before commencing the tests. Figure G.40 shows how all
specimens was measured and that all six sides of the cubes was photographed (sides

APPENDIX H
Purpose, Procedure, and
Expected Outcome of
Preliminary Experiments
This appendix is the product of one of the first supervisor meetings in which an early
version of the thesis statements was derived and the concepts of the experiments was
discussed. The final product changed somewhat in comparison to this appendix, but
this appendix still describes well the initial thoughts and expected outcome.
H.1
Determination of Early Strength Development in
SCC
H.1.1
Purpose
It is desired to determine the early strength development in a self-compacting concrete
in order to determine when it is able to support itself or better yet to determine when the
form pressure is decreasing. By early strength development means the time between 1 to
10 hours of maturity cf. Figure H.1 [Figure 18 in Portlandcementer, published in 1999
by Cementernes Tekniske Oplysningskontor]. The purpose of the experiment is thus to
clarify the connection between temperature development, maturity, and strength from
two different recipes of SCC. The recipes must be common and ideal for high vertical
casts such as walls. To avoid too many variables the aggregates must be the same at
the laboratory at DTU as the ones Unicon uses in the vicinity of Copenhagen.
The masters thesis will feature the following three elements:
1. Measurement methods and equipment for setting time for fresh SCC;
2. Measurement methods and equipment for the connection between temperature
development, maturity, and compressive strength;
3. Measurement methods and equipment for measuring form pressure - on the con-
struction site and in the laboratory.
==PAGE:210==
194
H Purpose, Procedure, and Expected Outcome of Preliminary Experiments
Figure H.1: Schematic presentation of the hardening of concrete as a function of time and
the corresponding progress of setting, strength development, heat generation, and change in
volume.
The following section describes the procedure for 2) Measurement methods and equipment
for the connection between temperature development, maturity, and compressive strength.
The other two elements 1) and 3) will be addressed later on.
H.1.2
Procedure
To begin with four forms are build in which three SCC cubes of 150 x 150 x 150 mm
can be cast in each. Each of the forms are isolated with 30 mm polystyrene. In the
center of the outer cube in each form a temperature wire are cast in. All four forms
are placed in a 75 mm polystyrene isolated box. In this way the hardening will take
place under as uniform conditions as possible. The temperature wires are connected to
a temperature-plotter which are able to log the development of the cubes in real time.
Connected to a PC the maturity is calculated instantly. When a maturity of 1, 2, 4,
6, 8, and 10 hours are reached two cubes are taken out of their forms and tested for
compressive strength according to the European standard DS/EN 12390-3. The cubes
==PAGE:211==
H.1 Determination of Early Strength Development in SCC
195
are tested in the order as shown in Figure H.2. Technical drawings of the forms and the
box are shown in Appendix L.
Figure H.2: Experimental setup of hardening of SCC cubes. The temperature development of
cube 3, 6, 9, and 12 are logged by cast-in temperature wires. The cubes are tested for compressive
strength in numerical order two-by-two after 1, 2, 4, 6, 8, and 10 hours of maturity respectively.
H.1.3
Expected Outcome
Based on the compressive strengths from the 12 cubes by six different hours of maturity
a connection can be plotted. Even though the early stage of the strength development
is investigated it is presumed that a tendency must apply which enable us to state a
hypothesis for which to continue with.

196
==PAGE:213==

APPENDIX J
Calibration of
Thermometers
During the experiments different types of thermometers have been used to measure devel-
opment of the air and the concrete temperature. The measurement of the temperature
have been used to correct the setting time and the accuracy of the instrument is there-
fore very important, why a calibration was done prior the calculation of the maturity of
the concrete. The used thermal equipment that have been used and calibrated are:
‚ÄĘ Four digital temperature loggers of the type ‚ÄĚTesto 176-T4‚ÄĚ with four channels
each into which thermo-elements can be connected. The Testo loggers have been
used to measure and save the temperature development with a interval of 1 min.
and are named Testo 1, Testo 2, Testo 3, and Testo 4.
‚ÄĘ One digital thermometer of the type ‚ÄĚThermometer T301‚ÄĚ manufactured by HBO
Instruments.
The calibration of the thermal equipment is done with a mercury-in-glass thermometer
with a scale ranging from 0 to 102¬įC and a scale division of 0.1 ¬įC. The mercury-
in-glass thermometer have been calibrated at the certified calibration laboratory for
measuring instrument of temperature DKD, Deutcher Kalibrierdienst. The correction
and measurement uncertainties at different temperature levels (20, 30, and 40¬įC) for the
mercury-in-glass thermometer is shown in Table J.1.1
Table J.1: Result of measurement for the calibration of the mercury-in-glass thermometer
done by Deutcher Kalibrierdienst DKD.

for all expected ranges of temperature. Each calibration was done with the setup shown

(a) An overview of the calibration setup of the
thermometers. From left: Digital thermometer of
the type ‚ÄĚthermometer T301‚ÄĚ, isolated container

Figure J.1: (a) left, (b) right
The calibration procedure is done as followed:
1. An isolated container with a volume of 5 liter of the brand Isoltherm from KGW
is filled with roughly 3 liter of demineralised water.
2. The mercury-in-glass thermometer is placed in the container with water and the
temperature of the water is noted.
3. The water is adjusted with cold or hot demineralised water to obtain a temperature
of respectively 20, 30 and 40 ¬įC (¬Ī 3¬įC)
4. The digital thermometer is placed in the isolated container with water.
5. One thermal cord is connected to Testo 1 channel 1.
6. The temperature is registered from all three displays after all thermometers have
been in the water for approximately two minutes. This to ensure that all ther-
mometers (digital and analog) have reached a stable value.
7. The registered temperature is noted for all three thermometers for the time called
‚ÄĚ0‚ÄĚ min.
8. The thermal cord connected to Testo 1 channel 1 is unplugged and connected to
Testo 1 channel 2.
==PAGE:219==
J Calibration of Thermometers
203
9. After 2 minutes a new registration of the three thermometers is done and noted.
This is done at a time called ‚ÄĚ2‚ÄĚ min.
10. The thermal cord connected to Testo 1 channel 2 is disconnected and connected
to Testo 1 channel 3.
11. After another 2 minutes a new registration of the three thermometers is done and
noted. This is done at a time called ‚ÄĚ4‚ÄĚ min.
12. The same procedure is done until all 16 channels for a total of 4 Testo devices
(Testo 4 channel 4) have been tested.
The results of all noted measurements for the three types of thermometers tested in
water with a temperature of respectively 20, 30, and 40¬įC can be seen in Figure J.2, J.4,
and J.6. The values from Figure J.2, J.4 and J.6 are plotted in Figure J.3, J.5, and J.7.

APPENDIX O
Visit at Unicon‚Äôs Concrete
Factory at Aved√łre
On February 22nd and March 17th Unicon‚Äôs concrete factory in Aved√łre was visited in
order to identify the fresh SCC quality control procedures of Unicon.
O.1
Introduction to Unicon‚Äôs Factory at Aved√łre
The concrete factory at Aved√łre is the busiest in Denmark, primarily due to it being
the primary deliverer of concrete for the Copenhagen Metro Expansion. The factory
is fully automated with regards to measuring moisture contents and mixing, and has
a manually test laboratory for checking the quality of the dispatched concrete. Before
every concrete truck leaves the factory its load is tested in the laboratory.
O.2
Quality Control of Fresh SCC
With every batch SCC the laboratory at the factory measures density, air content, and
flow diameter. If the costumer wants it - which the Metro Company wants for example -
also a V-funnel test and casts two √ł150/300 mm cylinders for compressive strength test
at 7 and 28 days of maturity respectively.
The procedures are as follows:
O.2.1
Density and Air Contents
A standard pressuremeter (Air Entrainment Meter) bucket is placed on a weight which
is zeroed. The bucket is filled with the fresh SCC. The weight is noted and knowing the
volume of the bucket the density of the SCC is calculated. Using the pressuremeter the
air contents is found in the fresh SCC.
O.2.2
Flow Diameter
A standard cone used for slump test is filled with fresh SCC. Unlike traditional concrete,
the SCC is not compacted with a rod in the cone. The top is leveled out and the cone
==PAGE:244==
228
O Visit at Unicon‚Äôs Concrete Factory at Aved√łre
lifted with a speed of approximately 2 seconds to lift it clear of the SCC. When the SCC
is done flowing the diameter is measured. Depending on the shape the SCC is measured
2-3 places and the mean is noted.
O.2.3
V-funnel Test
Unicon is a big supplier of the expansion of the Copenhagen Metro who have a require-
ment of a V-funnel test for their SCC deliveries. The V-funnel test, see Figure O.1
is used to control that the aggregates of the SCC are not too big and thus block the
passage. The V-funnel test is only done if the customer wishes it.
Figure O.1: V-funnel placed in the laboratory at Unicon‚Äôs concrete factory at Aved√łre.
O.2.4
Compressive Strength from Cylinders
Finally two cylinders with a diameter of 150 mm and a height of 300 mm are cast. These
are tested at 7 and 28 days of maturity respectively to determine their compressive
strength.

APPENDIX P
Supervisor Meetings
During the seven months of this masters thesis the authors met with their supervisors
seven times. At these meetings the outline of the thesis was laid, the focus was adjusted
to what the supervisors thought most interesting, and any number of questions regarding
the conduction of the experiments was clarified. The attached USB flash drive contain
all the agendas and the resumes of the meetings, and are thus an important source of
background knowledge regarding the authors‚Äô choice of course.
